show their written evidence of his success. All
communications should be addressed
Dr. RICHARDS,

Organisations of the Discontented.
SATURDAY, DECEMBER 13, 1890.
2.—SOCIALISM.
It is about one hundred and fifty years
since Rousseau said: “Were there no
luxury there would be no poor ” —a propo-
sition restated by the modern Socialist
thus : Were there no idlers all might enjoy
some measure of luxury ; for were society’s
tasks fairly apportioned, and the sources of
wealth open to all, then the absolutely-
necessary work would be performed so
speedily that many might be engaged in the
production of luxuries, and all would have
the leisure to cultivate a taste for refined
enjoyment. “ The fact that many men are
making clothes for one individual,” observed
Montesquieu, “ is the cause of there being
so many people without clothes.” Here the
Socialist again makes an emendation by
asserting that although inequitable distribu-
tion causes much poverty, that inadequate
production is the main source of the ever-
increasing flood of human misery ; that not
only are the clothes now made unfairly dis-
tributed, but farther, that clothes are not
made” in sufficient quantities to supply the
bodily ivants of the earth’s inhabitants. In
every country under the sun, even the
most densely-populated, cultivable land lies
fallow, ores await the alchemist who shall
transform them into the accessories of civi-
lised existence and the implements of pro-
duction, while houseless, ill-clad and hungered
men, women and children vainly cry aloud
in the market-place for shelter, raiment,
food and employment. The problem, there-
fore, is mainly how to increase production,
and chiefly that of food.
# * *
It has been asked : If poverty and its
attendant miseries can be traced back to the
monopolisation of land and the implements
of production in the hands of the Few, how
is it that the defrauded Many—invested with
moral right and physical might—have not,
long ere this, combined to bring about an
alteration for the better ? The question is
easily answered : Simply because their
poverty has induced a know-nothingness in
which they were easily persuaded by those
whose interests demanded a continuation of
their ignorance, that there always must be
hewers of wood and drawers of water, and
therefore always rich and poor ; that those
whom incapacity and lack of desire for better
things doom to drudgery, must always re-
main content to receive in return just as much
of the use-values created by their labour as
those who live at ease think proper to allot
them. The priest has unceasingly asserted
that, according to Divine dispensation, “ the
poor ye shall have with you always,” and
the economist leads up to the same conclu-
sion by stating that poverty is an unalterable
result of the irrevocable laws of nature.
Rebellion, therefore, on the first count, was
deemed impious ; on the second, illogical.
Nevertheless, revolutions have occurred.
Kings, priests, landlords and lawgivers, all,
to some extent, creatures of circumstances,
have been slain times without number ;
again and again, loudly denouncing they
knew not whom, have the proletary met,
conspiring against fate, to curse God and die
beneath the bayonets and buckshot of an
equally ill-informed authority. But the cries
of the discontented have heretofore been
inarticulate, and it is the aim of the modern
social reformer to apprise them of the source
of their misery and their power to eradicate
all that is now responsible for it. It was
first necessary, however, that they should
become normally discontented. The satisfied
pig remains a pig all his days : the drudge
satisfied with mere subsistence wages (the
hovel and coarse food that distends an easily-
satisfied stomach at the close of a long day’s
weary work) is doomed to an everlasting
drudgedom, which he hands down unim-
paired to his successors, embellished, in
some cases, by lingering starvation on in-
adequate wages, and, in extreme cases, by
absolute want of even the things necessary
to brutish existence. But education—the
education of the schoolroom, the library, the
newspaper article, the outdoor meeting and
the rough-and-ready teachings of bitter ex-
perience—is gradually exorcising the demon
of hoggish satisfaction, and all over the
world organisations of the discontented are
shaping the wail of the oppressed into
articulate speech and sounding the knell of
drudgedom.
* # *
Drilled by adversity, incited by suffering,
educated in spite of their task-masters, the
proletary begin to recognise that land and
labour are the only absolute necessities of
existence ; that wages are not paid by the
capitalist, but, on the contrary, that the
capitalist is subsidised by the wage-earner ;
that the employer does not advance wages
to the worker, but that the worker advances
labour to the capitalist ; that capital is not
the result of the exertions and abstinence
of the product-owner and property-holder,
but of the exertions and abstinence of the
producer ; that there is no conflict, no cause
for conflict between the labourer and capi-
tal, the animate and the inanimate,
the tradesman and the tool, but that the
disagreement is solely between the creators
of all wealth and its licensed appropriators.
They begin to see dimly that money is a
mere convenience of exchange, sometimes a
lying token of a wealth that has no real exis-
tence, an instrument in unscrupulous hands
for the enslavement of the masses, and that,
were all gold, silver and copper coin, bank-
notes, and bills destroyed, none of the
operations of nature and man would be sus-
pended ; that were all monopolistic restric-
tions --aye, and the paraphernalia of produc-
tion —annihilated, that only those would
perish to whom the creation of surplus-value
and the power to expropriate it, are neces-
sities of existence. “ Formerly the problem
was,” says G. H. Lewes, “ Given the welfare
and advantages of the Few, how best to re-
concile these with the welfare of the Many ?
It now is : Given the welfare of the Many
how best to secure the advantages of the
Few?” The problem of the immediate
future, declares the Socialist, is ; How may
wo best attain the welfare of All ?
* # *
Over-population is a relative condition. It
exists only because monopoly’s existence de-
mands the maintenance of a reserve labour
force. The greater the accumulations of the
wealthy, the greater the misery of the prole-
tariat and the larger the industrial reserve*
The inevitable result of production for pro-
duction’s sake, accumulation for accumula-
tion’s sake is that they constantly evolve —in
direct proportion to their hoardings—a
surplus army of drudges by whose aid wages
are kept down and the dependency of the
employe on the employer (the so-called state
or free contract existing between the holder
of the commodity, capital, and the owner of
the commodity, labour, so necessary to the
former’s dominance) is perpetuated, indeed
extended, for every increase of capital, in
monopolistic hands, is directly responsible
for an increase of the toilers’ burdens. Thus,
as statistics prove, two-thirds of the world’s
products are expropriated from the producer
as rent, interest, profits, insurance, broker-
age, &c.
# * *
Socialists do not propose an equal division
of the world’s wealth among the world’s in-
habitants, as the ignorant, whose sole notion
concerning Socialism is that furnished by
Marryatt’s “Equality Jack,” with an in-
evitable return to the former state of things,
suppose, but a State resumption of all natural
sources of wealth, and the jointly-created
instruments of production, in trust for all
the members of the community, compensat-
ing, in deserving cases, the dispossessed with
annuities. In fact, they do not intend to
patch up the present social system, but
gradually to construct a new one by
displacing competitive production for profit,
whose fundamental support is the appro-
priation of self-earned private property,
to make room for use-production by asso- ’
dated effort and a social system whose aim
is, not as now ; “ Each for himself and the
devil take the hindmost,” but “Each for all
and all for each.” In a community thus
organised, it will be essential that every
resident shall perform some useful office,
either with hand or brain, as producer, dis-
tributor, superintendent, administrator, in-
structor or amuser, while the results of each
man’s efforts shall belong absolutely to him-
self, except the portion necessary for the
replacement, diffusion and extension of the
capital which is common property. All
things will not be held in common, but all
that is necessary to the production of wealth
shall be common property, and production
will be carried on by united effort for univer-
sal benefit. Socialism, therefore, does not
aim at the substitution of one form of class
dominance for another, but at the annihila-
tion of all forms of class dominance and the
substitution of a community of interest, the
abolition of class selfishness and the substitu-
tion of a spirit which will strive for the bene-
fit of the entire human family.
# # *
Most Socialists advocate the carrying-on
of industrialism by the formation of trades-
groups in every district who would send
delegates to a central executive. The funds
necessary for communal purposes would,
probably, be provided by the payment of
royalties subscribed by the occupants of land
and the utilisers of national resources.
# * #
Railways, tramways and .other means of
transit, light and water supplying concerns
would, most likely, be controlled by the
municipalities whose domains they tra-
versed, and as industries in these days
would be run for use and not for profit, all
the return looked for would be a sum suffi-
cient to keep the concern in thorough work-
ing order.
# # •
Land, of course, would be nationalised
and, as the only recognised titles will be
occupancy and intelligent utilisation, all idle
portions, except those devoted to com-
munal purposes, will be open to free and
immediate entry.
* * «
Education in all its branches will be free
and secular, and, up to a certain age, com-
pulsory, because education is necessary to
amicable and useful existence, and because
the ignorant are the prey of those unscru-
pulous enough to make intellectual superi-
ority an instrument for the enslavement of
those not so favoured in mental equipment.
# # *
It is argued that in a community thus
organised, the disappearance of poverty and
the minimising of ignorance will remove
most of the incentives to crime and all the
hotbeds where it has been hitherto propa-
gated, and thus the administration of Justice
will cease to be a matter of such extent and
complexity. As Law is obviously intended
for the protection of the weak against the
encroachments of the strong, and not ac-
counted an article to be obtained in return
for prompt cash only, it will then be ad-
ministered free of cost by an elective
tribunal. Not only will there be no court
fees, there will be also no lawyer’s charges,
for the legal, like the judicial fraternity,
will be paid for the services they render the
community out of the common fund.
# * *
By a simple extension of the present
system, whereby museums, libraries, picture-
galleries, parks and baths, in most large
centres, are supported from the national
exchequer for the public benefit, it is pro-
bable, in a Socialistic State, that all places
of recreation will be national property, and
that the sole qualifications for admission will
be cleanliness and decorum. As entertain-
ments will not then be run for profit, seats
will not then be crowded together, galleries
will not be piled tier on tier, and there will
be no distinction as to the quality of the
sittings, little difference in the convenience
ot the position.
* # *
It was lately urged against Socialism by a
correspondent of the Evening News that the
formulations of its various propagandists
differ in many particulars. It is surely
evident that a social system is not to be
planned out and shapen like a coat or a
kitchen-table, but must be gradually beaten
into coherence and shape when heated at the
tire of public opinion by the hammer of per-
sonally-acquired experience on the anvil of
daily life.
* * *
It also was argued that the spread of edu-
cation and easy access to natural resources
would render itimpossible to obtain thosewho
should perform the community’s unpleasant
offices. But it is hardly necessary to doom
many millions of human beings to every
kind of misery in order that the supply of
scavengers may not fail. In a highly,
civilised state the use of scientific appliances
and chemicals will doubtless neutralise the
disagreeableness of the world’s dirty work.
Unpleasant duties may be allotted, however,
as punishment to evil-doers, or to those least
mentally-gifted, when labour is no longer
considered a degrading condition, their per-
formance will not be looked upon as deroga-
tory. It is certain that tire spread of
scientific education will make all kinds of
work more interesting and incalculably in-
crease the capabilities of mankind for intel-
ligent effort, instead of lessening the natural
desire to be of use.
* * *
Although hundreds of Socialists have
calmly resigned fortune and life in Russia
and elsewhere for Socialism’s sake, it is often
calmly cited as an objection that it is ordy
advocated by those who have nothing to lose,
or by the idle and impecunious for the pur-
pose of self-aggrandisement. Should a
wealthy Socialist be pointed out, however, it
is at once asserted that were he sincere he
would not remairr wealthy. To the first
attack the Socialist replies that the assump-
tion that the wronged have no right to de-
mand justice because they would personally
benefit by its being put into execution,
scarcely requires refutation. To the latter
charge his answer is that while competitive
production endures, the distribution of the
wealth held by wealthy Socialists among
their poorer brethren would only temporarily
relieve poverty and would not divert wealth
from the exploitation of labour. The money
owned by an honest Socialist, say they,
can best be laid out in propaganda work.
* # *
The sapient Evening News correspondent
asserts that in a Socialistic State, individual-
ism would be crushed, the reward of merit
entirely done away with, and that the intelli-
gent would then stand on an outward level
with their most ignorant neighbours. To
this the reply is that merit rarely receives its
full reward, indeed in thousands of cases is
wholly denied recognition ; that the bulk of
the inhabitants of any country have small
chance for the development of individuality
and are, for all useful purposes, as dependent
on the will of others as if chattel slaves ; that,
nowadays, the genius of the Haeckels,
Spencers and Tennysons does not gain for
them a tithe of the consideration freely
accorded to the gilded stupidity of
a Vanderbilt or a royal princeling ; that
in a Socialist State, the highest qualities
will receive the highest recognition, and that
the equality desired by Socialists is not a
supposititious equality of physical or mental
excellence, but of opportunity to live and
attain excellence and of evenhandedness in
the administration of justice. In any case,
pecuniary reward, alone, never incited any-
one to the performance of great or noble
deeds. Latent faculty demands for itself
cultivation and expression ; it will out, even
if there be no alchemists to transmute it
into precious metals, or seats in high places.
* # *
Again —still quoting from the News’ paper-
spoiler—it is asserted that “ Socialism will
be an incentive to idleness, for the result
(sic) of industry is done away with when a
father cannot transmit anything towards the
maintenance of his family after his decease.”
That is to say : Jones will deliberately
starve himself and family during his life-
time, because he cannot support them after
his death ; a line of argument that would
have adherents nowhere, if not at Glades-
ville. Passing over the absurdity of this
wildly illogical supposition, it maybe pointed
out that, could human beings be thus affected,
the world’s work would be at a standstill,
for not one worker in a thousand has the
slightest chance of transmitting anything of
value to his heirs at decease. In a Socialistic
community, however, the dying parent may
with confidence leave his or her little ones
to the charge of a State that gladly assumes
the parentage of all who are young and
helpless.
# # *
Further, Socialism will be a direct incen-
tive to industry, for whereas many are now
entirely exempt from useful exertion, then
it will be absolutely necessary that all,
except the physically and mentally incapaci-
tated, shall work if they would eat.
* * #
Another objection is, that an extension of
State duties would entail an increase of State
corruption. To this it is replied that now
everything is governed by commercialism,
that profit is the first law of existence, and
that even principles are bought and sold, but
that in the organisation which is to be, com-


mercialism will have small place, corruption
slippery foothold. Now the State is master
of the People ; then, it will be only an ex-
pression of the People’s will.
# * *
The basis of Socialism, therefore, is co-
operative effort attended by distributive
justice. To recapitulate : A State thus con-
stituted will control natural resources and
reproductive forces—the means of produc-
tion and distribution of wealth—but will not
interfere with the disposition of self-earned
private property. Herein it differs widely
from Communism which aims at the control,
not only of the instruments and manner of
production and distribution, but absolutely
of all the produce. In fact every Communist
is a Socialist and something more, for the
motto of the former is: “To each according
to his needs,” while the latter’s is: “To
each according to his deeds,” or, “ No rights
without duties, no duties without rights.”
PLAIN ENGLISH.
England’s Goodness.
Speaking recently on the Federation question,
Minister Bruce Smith, of the New South
Wales Cabinet, rose to grease the old chariot of
loyalty:—
England protects us without any cost with her army and
navy, supported by the poor of England ; and if England
asked us to contribute as much for its interests as it con-
tributes to us, we should probably turn round and insult
the Crown of England. Why, England does everything
for us!
It is time that this miserable old yarn had a
rest. England, as Mr. Bruce Smith ought to
know, does not protect Australia with her
army, for the said army is not here, neither
would it be likely to get here in time if it were
wanted, and in any case it wouldn’t come
whether it was wanted or not, for it is all
required to keep down Nationalism in Ireland,
and to collect tithes in Wales, and to jump on
the hungry mob in London and India, and it is
in a state of chronic mutiny into the bargain.
And supposing it did come it would do so for
the purpose of fighting England’s enemies, not
the enemies of Australia, for this country has
none ; and consequently it would be attending
exclusively to its own business. Moreover,
England’s army is not altogether supported by
the “ poor of England,” seeing that it is one-
half Asiatics supported by the poor of India,
and as the poor of Australia keep up, in pro-
portion to their number, quite as large a force
as the rest of the Empire, Bruce Smith’s sym-
pathy is utterly misplaced. Further, the
British navy, so far as it is expended
in these waters, is employed to defend,
not) Australia, but British commerce in
the Australian seas, and this country is no
more obligated to pay for this service than
Brazil or the Argentine republic to pay for the
cruisers which haunt their shores for a precisely
similar object. Notwithstanding which Aus-
tralia does pay a yearly contribution towards
the support of a British squadron, and makes a
conspicuous ass of herself by so doing because
England would have to keep the ships there in
any case. The only service which the Austra-
lias ever received from any British force was
the massacre of the diggers at Ballarat, and the
kindly assistance of that gang of brigands
known as the New South Wales Corps, and the
Mother Country has never yet paid for the
breakages committed by those two hordes—
neither, apparently, does she intend to. No
doubt there is a possibility that any further
attempts at exaction by the Imperial Govern-
ment might lead the Australian people to “turn
round and insult the Crown of England,” but
as the said Crown supplies neither the army
nor the navy, and does not defend Australia or
any other place, there is a considerable lack of
relevancy in this observation. Certainly the
fact that the “ poor of England ” bear the chief
burden of the forces maintained in England, while
the Crown grabs all the best offices for its relatives
is sufficient justification for any insults that
may be levelled at that article of furniture, but
as the Crown keeps on its way unmoved it
would seem that up to date it had not been in-
sulted anything like so much as it requires.
And finally the Minister’s declaration that
England “does everything” for Australia is
about as wildly impossible as the assertion
that England has boiled the North Pole, for no
country mentioned in history or tradition ever
“ did everything” for another, and in addition
no country ever did anything for another except
in the hope that her own interests would some-
how be served thereby.
Consistent Piety,
“British society,” says the Age, apropos to
the awful smash of that eminent co-respondent
Parnell, “is sincerely and consistently pious.”
The Bulletin comes on deck to remark, in a
hoarse, muffled tone, that it cordially endorses
this sentiment. Piety is the strong point of
the British nation, and it is a hog for morals
and humanity. When it hears of a perishing
sinner anywhere its soul is stirred right down to
its boots, and it collects money from people who
are starving to send a missionary to that sinner,
and when the missionary is eaten it rises in
pious indignation and despatches an army
which hangs the perishing heathen right off for
his iniquity, and annexes his country, and des-
patches his soul—if he hag a soul—to hell—if
there is a hell. Then the army comes back,
and when the nation hears that they are starv-
ing its pious fervour rises to fever-heat, and the
police nobly run the veterans in for vagrancy,
and the Church wakes up in a blaze of holy in-
dignation and supplies them with tracts in gaol.
Also, a subscription is raised, and somebody
embezzles it; Now and then, too, some paper
like the famous Pall Mall Gazette unearths a
great national infamy and publishes it in all its
unclean details, and the piety of the Empire
gets up on its hind legs to such an alarming
extent that twenty-four streets are blocked by
the crowd which is rushing to buy the story.
Or else the country sends out a pious fanatic
like the famous Gordon to carry the balm of
a spiritual Gilead into the dark places of
the earth, and that apostle in a coat of mail
with an explosive tail proceeds to whop the
tribes who refuse to listen to his message, and
the balm of Gilead blows up and kills every-
body in sight. And again the sympathetic
nation learns that some 3,000,000 of its own
brothers and sisters are living in chronic star-
vation, and in obedience to the divine law of
charity it resolves to chuck them out into a far
country, and the inhabitants of that country
generally reply that they will see all Britain
hanged first, and much trouble ensues. The
British nation is sincerely and consistently
pious, and that is just what is the matter
with it, for its piety generally kills somebody,
and the good tidings have a tendency to explode
and lift the roof off.
The contemplated introduction to Queensland
of cheap Italian labourers as substitutes for the
Polynesians is not likely to commend itself to
the democrats of the Northern Colony. The
contention of the white workers has all along
been that unless sugar-growing can pay for its
production on a similar basis to wheat-growing,
wine-growing, gold-mining, timber-getting, and
the other thousand and one budding resources
of a young country, it had better be given up.
The sugar-planters, on the other hand, falsely
allege that their industry gives direct and
indirect employment to 50,000 white men, in
consequence of the efforts of from 8000 to 9000
subsidiary coloured workers —blacks and
Chinese, &c. But even if this were the case,
the white workers would prefer a smaller
amount of employment to be performed ex-
clusively by white men. In the black-labor
districts the frequent outrages by kanakas, the
drunken street orgies, and the Chinese fan tan and
opium dens which spring up for the allurement
of the South Sea Islander, have long been a
menace and a terror to the white residents ;
the lock-ups are full of “ the cheap and
reliable,” and the Police Court exists chiefly to
right the wrongs inflicted on or by him.
Hence, at the present stage, the white worker
has determined to rid himself of the incubus
once and for ever. The sugar-planter has
received more than his due meed of considera-
tion at the hands of his country, and his
industry has been, from time to time, the sub-
ject of more legislative pampering than any
other private enterprise. He has had Kanakas,
Cingalese and Javanese specially indented for
him ; £50,000 of State money irretrievably lost
in a silly Central mill experiment on his
behalf—and all within the space of ten years.
The introduction of Kanaka labour will cease
by law at the end of the present month, but the
planter may use such labour as he has on that,
date for a further term of from one to three
years. That will give him ample time to make
some fresh arrangements. The “Italian”
scheme, however, will prove a certain failure,
and no one knows this better than the planter
himself. The best plan, and the only one which
possesses the possibilities of success, is for the
planter to make up his mind to the employment
of white labour, either upon a co-operative or a
contracting basis. His graceful acceptance of
an inevitable situation will do more for the
Queensland sugar-planter in the present crisis
than any amount of futile “ kicking against the
pricks.”

Alfred Bennett prints in Sydney Evening
News a most interesting article (nearly a column
long) about a prize-fight which took place in
—New Jersey!—between “twolovely daughters
of two prominent and wealthy families,” who
“ stripped off to a fighting rig” to square their
little differences, and whose ‘ ‘ well-rounded
forms became a mass of bruises and blood.”
Alfred tells us that when the combatants met
to fight about their man,
A modest girl gasped and said: —
“ You are not going to take off everything, are you ?”
“ Why, of course,” said Minnie. “ Don’t be silly.”
And they did, down to the waist.
The boys who were peeking blushed.
Then Alfred goes on to say that in the ring
Mabel led off first with a blow straight from the
shoulder. Minnie dodged this and let fly one in return
with her right. It did not land, for Mabel got away too
quick. There was blood in the eyes of both girls and
they struck out viciously. Blow followed blow, and the
girls began to get winded. Time was called and they,
retired to their corners.
When time was called again they stepped up briskly
and went at it hammer and tongs.
They punched each other’s fair shoulders and breasts
for 27 rounds without drawing blood, and then they got
mad and clinched. They clawed and scratched and pulled
out hair. Two tigresses could not have been more
wicked. The air was full of black locks and golden
tresses, and blood was flowing in little rivulets down a
white background, &c., &c.
Well, well ! What curious things some news-
papers have to do to get a living in these dull
times !

Perhaps the most remarkable feature of the
late strike in Queensland was the determined
manner in which the entire Press of the pro-
vince endeavoured to “ down ” the men. Even
now the men cannot understand the riddle,
simple though it be. The fact is, the Brisbane
Courier dominates the provincial press ; it
supplies over two-thirds of the press telegrams,
and thus tinges the opinions of its constituents
on all questions, political, economic, or social.
The late Thady O’Kane’s wired remonstrances
to this kind of thing were characteristic and
funny.
—* ♦
Profound slab of thought from the S.M.
Herald :
The efficiency of the public service is not promoted, bub
is likely to suffer, when deserved promotion and the
recognition of merit are made subordinate to favoritism
or political influence, or mistaken belief that it is neces-
sary, in filling the best places, to look abroad and appoint
unknown men.
This is a great question, but, as usual, the
Hei iald takes a purely superficial view of the
subject. The real danger lies in the circum-
stance that favoritism, or political influence, is
exercised in a manner that is calculated to
deteriorate the moral atmosphere of the subject,
under the agency of an erroneous conviction
that some unspecified individual ought to receive
considerable remuneration at frequent intervals
without performing any adequate exertion to
counterbalance the unwonted exuberance of his
honorarium, thereby leading to a morbid con-
dition in which the principal labors of the
department have to be executed by persons who
are unjustly deprived of any reasonable
pecuniary compensation for the abnormal wear
and tear incident upon the situation. Con-
sequently, the latter class, in the absence of any
adequate prospective increment, fails to develope
and manifest that intelligent interest in problems
of administration which would characterise
their proceedings under the influence of more
favourable surroundings, whereby the inherent
abortiveness of existing arrangements becomes
still more strongly accentuated. We now pro-
ceed to take a well-earned rest.
When the Assembly at Adelaide proposed to
congratulate West Australia on the introduc-
tion of constitutional government there was one
dissenting voice—only one. One Tomkinson
rose and stated in a thick, fat voice that there
was nothing—absolutely nothing—to congratu-
late about. Government by the people, he
said, was a failure, and liberty wasn’t up to
much, and elective parliaments were a God-
forsaken contrivance; and then the old man’s
voice faltered, and he dropped a tear over the
memory of the days that are gone —the simple,
primitive days, when Britain simply sent out a
governor, and a judge, and a hangman, and a
great, brawny, upstanding gaol-flogger, and
these four ran Australia among them. Where
had that quiet patriarchal age gone to, Tomkin-
son wanted to know, and what had the world
got in its stead? Practically nothing. Religion
had gone to blazes, and low, common people
went fooling with afFail's of State, and nobody
respected anybody, and the masses weren’t
kept down as they ought to be, and nobody was
flogged half so much as he needed to bring him
to a due sense of his inferiority. It was an age
of sin and license, and devoid alike of order and
veneration, and when it was proposed to con-
gratulate an adjoining colony because it had
also cast off all wholesome restraint, his voice
—the voice of Tomkinson —would be lifted in
denunciation of the unholy proposal. And then
the old man sat down heavily in the dust and
refused to be comforted.
The Recorder of Portsmouth, England, has just
sent a woman who stole a blanket, the property
of her mother-in-law (and pawned it to get
food), to gaol for five years; and has given
another woman, 65 years of age, seven years’
penal servitude for stealing a second-hand shirt
valued at Is. 6d. Sir William Hardman, for
20 years chairman of the Surrey Sessions, has
just died, and has no doubt been received into
Heaven with great applause. He sent 777 per-
sons into penal servitude ; the aggregate total
of the sentences imposed was 5,826 years, or an
average of 7J years each ! On June 6, 1871,
Maria Thomas, aged 54, was indicted for steal-
ing 41b. of beef, value Is. Bd. The accused,
who was a married woman, and the mother of
10 children, had been twice previously con-
victed, once for stealing a piece of bacon value
35., for which she was sentenced to four months,
and next, for stealing a shirt, value Is. 3d, to
12 months. These convictions were duly
proved before Sir William Hardman, who
sentenced her to seven years’ penal servitude,
and seven years’ police supervision. Her hus-
band committed suicide a few days after the
trial. At the same sessions, a lad of 18
(whose parents had both been convicted)
was indicted for stealing a metal watch-
guard, value 3s. The only “previous con-
viction ” against this lad was four years’
detention in a reformatory school, and with
tears in his eyes he pleaded for mercy and an
opportunity of “ turning over anew leaf.”
Sir William Hardman told him “he had
heard such tales befox-e,” and sentenced him
to seven years’ penal servitude and seven years’
police supervision. The barrister who defended
the lad remarked, “ He’s a boy, my lord.” “ I
do not need to be reminded of that, sir,”
answered Sir William severely, and the
counsel collapsed. Hardman gave a servant
girl 20 years of age 10 years’ penal servitude
for receiving, as a token of affection, a ring
which her lover (a burglar whose occupation she
did not know, so far as the evidence disclosed)
had stolen. It is almost a pity that a man
capable of such infamous cruelties should have
been allowed by those whom poverty drove to
the commission of petty thefts to die in his bed.
W. H. Groom has got the sugar-planters
of Queensland collectively “on their ear” by
stating that the 55,000 acres in that colony
devoted to cane-growing are not under cultivation.
During the general election of 1880 Parnell
put forth powers of endurance and hard work that
astonished those who came in contact with him.
At that time ‘ ‘ Tim ” Healy, a perfectly unknown
man, was his private secretary. During the
crisis of an election in a Southern constituency
Parnell was working with the local committee in
the small hours of the morning when Healy
entered the room where they were, completely
weary after a long day’s work. Parnell had been
urged to lie down on a sofa in the room and take
some rest. He was about to do so, but seeing
Healy’s fatigue made the latter occupy the sofa
instead. As the morning advanced the weather
became very cold. Then Parnell, excusing him-
self, rose from the table where the committee was
at work, and fetching his great ulster, folded it
ax-ound the sleeping form of his secretary. He
tlxen returned to business. Healy owes much
more than this to Parnell, wherefore his recent
conduct somehow looks ugly.
The N.S.W. Labour Commission will be a
huge and costly farce. Its appointment was
Parkes’ method of temporarily pushing an in-
terminable question out of the political arena.
The cunning old man offered to pay out of the
Treasury those members of Parliament engaged
on the Commission, if the Opposition would
assent; but the bait, the acceptance whereof
would have placed a number of Oppositionists in
the pay of the Government, was declined. Dr.
Garran, ex-editor of the S.M. Herald, is presi-
dent, and remarks that, as the Commission is only
to be in existence three months, it can’t possibly
get much information from abroad, and so must
fall back on the books locally available. The idea
of a dozen or more commonplace persons, seven-
eighths of whom couldn’t think in a straight line
for two minutes upon any mentionable subject,
settling a question as old the world, is too
ludicrous. The Bulletin’s recommendation is
that the Commission be squashed at once, and
tlxat Dr. Garran be asked to write for publication
a leading-article on the subject at a cost not
exceeding three guineas.

In his old age Chief Justice Lilley, of
Queensland, is becoming a powerful advocate of
temperance. Temperance, as Judge Lilley under-
stands it, doesn’t mean teetotalism. It only
means adapting the length and strength of your
potations to the hardness of your head. Lilley’s
head is made of chilled nickel-steel.

Griffith, however, being a spare man of cold un-

better than Mac., whose liver occasionally makes
him sit up. On no festive occasion has Sam.
Griffith been observed to turn a hair.
The criminal libel-suit instituted during
the late strike by Parson Brentnall, of Brisbane
Telegraph, against Mabbot, Secretary of the
Federated Wharf Labourers’ Union, bids fair to
fizzle out, as the Crown declines to bear the cost
of prosecution, and the Rev. Brentnall himself
loves money just as well as he loves revenge.
H. Vincent West, who acted as agent for
the Halles, is a son of the famous Quaker-Chartist
lecturer, Henry Vincent, editor of the Bath
Vindicator, who all his life, with voice and pen,
fought the battle of radicalism, and suffered im-
prisonment for upholding principles that to-day
are, almost without exception the law of the land.
The oldest pensioner of N.S. Wales has
just died, in the person of ex-trooper Thomas
Bevan, who, since the Ist July, 1850, had drawn
sixpence per day, or £9 2s 6d per annum, in
recognition of his old-time service. Now that
Bevan has departed, the oldest pensioner on the
list is ex-trooper Edward Wilson, of Penrith,
who for almost forty years has drawn £l4 8s 3d
per annum.
Years ago Jack Annear, of Maryborough
(Q.), was a contractor on the Gympie railway,
and the employer of G. S. Casey, the present
organiser of the Queensland Labour Federation.
Casey now twits Annear with having paid him
2s. a day extra to make the pace hot for the
other men in the same gang. Annear gives the
retort courteous by saying: “That shows you
were always a blanky dog !” Yet Annear still
travels on the Labour ticket when he can.

May Jordan, the young, refined and well-
educated woman who has undertaken the
organisation of the female workers of Brisbane, is
a daughter of the late Henry Jordan, ex-Minister
for Lands. Since she engaged in her present
work, Miss Jordan has received the cut direct
from many of her old “friends,” who vilify her
in the most unmeasured terms. Still she perse-
veres, and ; notwithstanding present opposition,
will certainly succeed in realising what she deems
to be her mission.
Froude’s “ Life of Beaconsfield,” 260 pp.,
is just out. An interesting part is about Mrs.
Willyaras, a little old Torquay widow, who left
Dizzy enough money to buy Hughenden Manor.
He first met her at the Crystal Palace in 1851,
when she slipped a note into his hand. ’Twas a
£IOOO-oheque, though it remained neglected in his
coat-pocket for weeks, as he thought the woman
was “balmy.” Afterwards she became the chief
friend of Mr. and Mrs. Disraeli, and Dizzy wrote
her many charming letters.
“ Bob ” Philp, junior member for Towns-
ville in the Queensland Assembly, was a clerk in
the Brisbane firm of Bright Bros, less than a
dozen years ago. Thence he went north to
James Burns, a Townsville grocer, and finally
bought out his employer. Later on, Burns and
Philp again met and established the present
octopean concern which bears their names. Philp
is a self-contained and'shrewd business man, a
Freetrader and an ardent apostle of black labour.
Burns is simply a canny Scot who possesses the
national acquisitiveness in abnormal degree.
Together they have pretty well annexed N.Q.
Says Pall Mall ;
Lord Oarington has been an excellent and popular
Governor, with a tact and geniality which suited colonial
audiences to aT. But there is an amusingly latter-day
touch about the language in which at Wagga he has been
commending his successor to N.S. Wales. Lord Jersey,
according to Lord Oarington’s testimonial, is “honest,
straightforward, conscientious, filled with energy, and
full of the wish to do what is right.” Then comes the
popular touch. “He has also,” declares Lord Carington,
with empressement, “plenty of money and not one atom
of ‘side.’” The colonial Governor fin de siede (it’s no
use trying to pension off that phrase till a.d. 1900) has
but one ambition, that it may be written on his tomb—
HE HAD LOTS OF MONEY
AND NOT AN ATOM OF SIDE.
Crick has again been triumphantly re-
turned to the N.S.W. Assembly. No other result
could be expected from a contest in the bush
between a bright and eloquent, however violent,
young man, and a prosy dullard, however vir-
tuous, who read his hustings speech to a dis-
gusted audience. The N.S.W. press howls for
Respectability, for what Crick epigramatically
calls “Purse and Belly,” in Parliament; but
rather than countenance the return of more -repre-
sentatives of that “pig-vote” class which has for
go many years swinishly supported Parkes through
thick and thin with such disastrous results to the
country, the bush electors are prepared to see men
enter the N.S.W, Legislative Assembly flourishing
the reeking, newly-lifted scalps of his enemies.
George the Fourth’s little Lady Jersey was
the chief instrument in working his marriage with
Queen Caroline, because she foresaw that this
woman would disgust him. Lady Jersey was
Caroline’s cicerone to England, and insulted her in
every possible way. George IV. became acquainted
with one of his other harem-scarems, Lady Hert-
ford, in an oblique manner. First of all he wanted
his mistress (nay, his wife), Mrs. Fitzherbert, to
be entrusted with the care of Lady H. 's niece, and
negotiations about this led to his falling into the
treacle, this villainous blow-fly. The above is all
frankly stated in Lord Holland’s Memoirs of the
Whig Party ; and then there was Lady Conying-
hara, that sweet toothpiece, who was said to be
not altogether unconnected with the appointment
of Sumner as Archbishop of Canterbury. All
that sort of thing is done now, Why? Because
human nature has changed. N.B. -- i The Peeresses
never angled for George IV. Jersey, Hertford,
and Conyngham were mysterious exceptions—
strange women who had got in. Yet none of
them had been opera-dancers or actresses.

840 Macquarie-street North, Sydney, Dec. 10,1890.
Mr Dear Moorabinda,—
It is being generally remarked in more than one
“kawliny” that the Australian-born wives of itinerant
Anybodies are ever so much more offish than their im-
ported ancestors. “A prophetess is not without honour
save in her own country,” and the woman who feels con.
scions that the entire assembly are well aware that her
aunt took in washing naturally behaves with an embar-
rassing hauteur, intended to keep dangerously-reminis-
oency people at an arm’s length, perhaps.
At a recent N.S.W. Cabinet meeting, it was decided that
an additional storey shall be added to Admiralty House,
North Shore, which is a charming little villa residence for
retired trade, but hardly imposing enough to house “ His
Excellency” Lord Charles Scott when he isn’t being
wobbled about on the mighty deep. The drawing-room,
for instance, isn’t large enough to swing a cat in. The
alterations will he effected while the family are in New
Zealand. I hope due care will be taken in the building of
it. For one thing, all dusty marine rules and regulations
should be hunted up, lest the wrongful slant of a shingle
or the unorthodox hack view of a brick should be
construed as a vulgar attempt to undermine the dignity
of “ His Excellency.” By the way, I see they are erect"
ing a vulgar hide-store right next door. This, I presume,
will involve a special Parliamentary grant for lavender-
water in midsummer.
It is well known that Lord Charles Scott would like to
tuck his fleet under his arm and found “ the Station ” in
Hobson’s Bay, or forninst the gasometer in the Yarra, and
leave Farm Cove and Potts Point in howling wilderness,
just to “learn us,” as it were. The Admiraless doesn’t
exactly throw cold water on hubby’s raging desire to snub
Sydney for refusing to let him hang from the chandelier
as patron-saint of the whole mob of that historic feed, and
unless the incensed deity—l mean the dignitary—lets his
anger cool, local belledom will have to fall back on the
vows of the bank dude and the declarations of the heir of
the wholesale drapery, just in the old sweet way.
“ Jarsey ” is learning that hanky-panky “ like a bird.’
At a banquet tendered him at Oxford the other day, he
said that he “ was not going into exile, but only to a part
—perhaps rather a distant part—of the same great
empire, peopled by men of the same race, with the same
kindly feeling and the same generous instincts.” Poor
Jarsey! and the worst of it is he will probably believe
himself a prophet, for the Government House haunter is
an adoring creature, who would walk on his hands if
Jersey locomoted in that manner, and who will model
himself generally on that imminent vice-regal strawberry
blond, perhaps to the extent of dyeing his flowing locks
terra-cotta. Jersey won’t have to consort with anything
very different from what he leaves behind him, except,
perhaps, that the “ English ” accent will fly off at the
handle in unguarded moments, and that he will have to
remember not to take it as a matter of course that every-
body has had a grandfather. The noble Earl left England
for Sydney on sth inst.
Jersey is to be a “conservative” Governor who will
draw the line, yer know. In which case an unalterable
parti carr6 will be the only possible festivity at Govern-
ment Owse.
Burlesques are not alvmys funny, by a long way, but
“ All Baba,” as rendered by the curled darlings of H.M.S.
Cordelia, at Sydney New Opera House, on 2nd inst., was
most amusing right through. Lieutenant AY. P. Hillyar
as Ali (and stage-manager, too) had an excellent make-up
—shrouding his fatal beauty under a false nose and an
egg-bald head—and acted and warbled with a winning
Snazelle-like solemnity which made an instant hit.
Lieutenant Woolcombe was Hussaiac, and had evidently
not plotted his face make-up for the allurement of the
stage - boxes, nevertheless he was a fascinating
villain, and he and Ali were a brace of loveable
suspicious characters, without whom the entertainment
had fallen very flat W hen either of them launched out
on one of the innumerable topical songs and parodies—
.smartly localised here and there—one could hear a person
thinking out in the street the hush was so great, and the
applause they always received showed that the audience
was manually appreciative, even if it was more than
half feminine. There wasn’t a gal on the stage until the
last act (three somewhat wooden midshipmen playing the
Incidental ladies—Cogibaba, Mr. C. E. Carver, in pale-blue
Turkish bagleto being the girliest of the trio), and then
a round dozen of Sydney beauty—as ‘-‘guests, thieves,’’
&o.—danced the waltz cotillon-very correctly, the effect
being rather spoilt by one or two of the dresses, which
were scarcely festive enough, though Miss Broomfield’s
“ Dorothy ” frock was very fresh and pretty. The dance
was encored—why, though, did they go all through it
again?—and at the finish Ali and Hassaiac presented
“souvenir” sprays of roses and ferns to the dancers, who
will doubtless cherish them fondly till—till the next war-
ship coihfes in.
The “ house ” was very good. The circle and stalls were
full, and Sir Charles -and Lady Scott occupied the front
seats in the circle. In the semi-gloom that prevailed qne
could hardly identify anybody, much less their outer
garments, but Lady Scott was distinct, in a black gown
with a cluster of flowers on the V-shaped corsage ; Miss
Ryan (her sister), in pale-blue silk with full sleeves of
chiffon and hoops of silver braid; Lady Innos (accom-
panied by Sir George lanes), in artistic sunflower-silk in
brown silk with panel of yellow silk, sleeves of yellow
chiffon, pearl necklace and diamond brooch ; Miss Lines,
electric-blue silk made en bebc; Miss Bight, who was
with a swarm of peop'e in the prompt d.c.-box, in black
with ruffles of melon-pink chiffon; Mrs. Gordon, in a
becoming gown of black lace lit-up by a scarlet fan 1
Lady Martin, in black rep silk, the square front filled in
with soft white muslin ; the Misses Martin, in simple
white frocks with belts of corded white ribbon and silver
buckles : Mrs. Forbes Angus, in black silk and laoe. Full
dross was worn in the reserved stalls, also, and, indeed,
the house was so altogether lovely that each of the gifted
officers must have smiled a gratified smile as he applied
an anxious eye to the peep-hole on the prompt side
before the curtain went up. The proceeds of this most
joyous entertainment arc to be (levoted to the Children’s
Hospital.
A( this ebullition of marine histrionics, a well-known
Sydney matron in the circle, desirous of changing to a
seat ahead, philanthropically refrained from brushing
past the knees of a dozen people, and took an airy short
cut across the back of the chair in front of her. It was
remarked that the hose were black, clocked, and well-
lit ting ; but it was all over in the twinkle of an eyelash.
And, speaking of the doings of the “dress"-division,
ever since the evanishment of Lady Carington, who
always gave the deserving a “hand,” a strong silence as
of yore has fallen over the circle, which presents the
puzzling anomaly of absolute boredom, combined, how-
ever, with a fixed intention to “ sit it out.” This in-
ter;sting phase of “dignified” social fiddlesticks is only
observable in its full beauty on a really “ fashionable ”
night.
On the heels of the disappearance of cosmopolitan Cora
will probably follow the abandonment of Potter manners,
lace to the knuckles, the scoop bang, draperies con-
structed mainly in the interests of lower outline. Society
will take to brushing its hair ; it will not say “ thart ” for
“ that ” nor “lomp”for “lamp.” It will not cultivate
an erotic underlip, it will not practise suggestive leers
with a hand-mirror, as I am told one devoted copyist of
the Potter’s meaning grin was, and is in the habit of
doing - . The reason why a certain set of women seize upon
the styles of a “professional beauty” like Mrs. B.P. is
because she is a success as sich, and they imagine that by
following in her wide footprints—while she Is the rage—
they may win admiration through the same exterior
methods. But they forget it doesn’t all lie in the recipe.
What is the use of the Potter snigger over bad teeth ? Or
the Nellie Stewart moue on a monkey mouth ?
It is most edifying to follow the applause bestowed on
the genius who plays the violin. He may evolve dainty
difficult melody—sweet birds full of “ technique’’—but
the public sits with idle palms until it hears something as
if it were giving the performer internal cramps, and then
it signifies its approval out loud. The frequency with
which he lets the really difficult movement slip and works
himself up into a frenzy over a showy musical catch-
phrase is one of the most interesting points about the
Philistine.
I am reminded that there is at least one Sydney artist
who must feel more at ease in public since the departure
of Lord Carington. The genius in question certainly
does wave to and fro like a reed shaken in the wind when
he is seated at the piano, but that was no reason why the
Baron should have invariably imitated him to the life for
the benefit—well, really, for the benefit of the mixed
audience, who generally kept an eye glued to the vice-
regal goings-on. And Lord O.’s engaging way of carrying
on a waggish conversation while the incensed genius was
punishing the ivories is another reason why the musician
doesn’t shed a tear over the memory of the Ist November.
The grand operatic festival given to Monsieur Deslouis
at the Y.M.C.A. Hall on the Cth inst., was attended by a
fair audience, mostly in evening dress - a German lady in
web-like white lace, with a trail of blush roses from the
left shoulder and a filmy scarf of white chiffon about her
shoulders looking soothingly dream-maidenish; and
Carrie Swain was there in blue silk with sleeves and ruffles
of blue chiffon. Monsieur Deslouis was in good voice and
got a “sympathetic” reception. Madame Kellerman and
Kowalski were brought back after their concerto on two
pianos. Mademoiselle Bestel—a pupil of Deslouis—made
her debut, and was gaspingly nervous ; and Van Iperen,
the blond fallen-from-the-skies sort of fiddler, kept up a
swaying motion all the time he was fiddling that made
your Sappho feel seasick. Both the lady-performers were
handed flowers with tri-coloured streamers—the gentle-
men getting their applause “ penny plain,” as usual.
We are in a hot summer, and so zephyrs, muslins and
prints will be the go. I can picture the happy smile
under the moustache of the benedict as he reads the sunny
prophecy—but let him smile on the other side of his face.
Proper print frocks are not cheap. It's over the “cut”
and the “ extras” and the finishing that the shillings run
into the pounds. She who likes to have her frocks as nice
inside as they are out goes in for silk-lined hems, even
though the garment is calico. I saw a very natty gown-
finished for a recent bride—which is an instance of the
way “it” mounts up. The material was china-blue
batiste with a white polka dot; the skirt plainly gored
with three tiny frills right round ; the bodice pinafore
draped over a yoke of shirred white mull; the gigot
sleeves slashed over at the elbow over white mull: a knot
of blue and white ribbons falling from the left hip. It
was very pretty—but so was the bill—four guineas ! To
give the skirt the proper clinging hang, the reckless
frockist had built it on a silk foundation.
With print frocks only the simplest head-gear will be
sported—a straw sailor hat with a plain belt of ribbon
according best with the style. Some of the sailor hats:
have roses or pansies or any large single flower placed at-
intervals round the brim, but the plain ribbon is the
neatest and most ladylike trimming. I saw a tiny sailor
hat the other day starred round with sunflowers.
A fair and fragile Melbourne young man had a dreadful
experience with his best girl the other day. It seems
that they had been parted for six long months, and a&
the weary days dragged by he felt that life without Fairy
was a useless thing and that he must have one more-
glance into her sunlit eyes if it cost him a month’s screw’
So he got a holiday and started for the back-blocks next-
morning. He was ushered into the boarding-house
sitting-room, and after a moment’s pause the door burst
open with a bang, and he understood afterwards his
darling had thrown herself into his hungry arms. He
didn't know it at the time, because she knocked him
heel over|tip over the centre table on to the cat, which
vanished up the chimney with a vindictive shriek,
and when he came to, his future mother-in-law was
bathing the bumps with arnica while the darling collected,
the broken china from the floor. She had put on seven
pounds of weight at that hotel and he wasn’t prepared
for it -that was all.
Which somehow reminds me of a butterfly youth who
once confided to me a sensational incident in his career.
It was cruel, but very human. He said ; “ I thought I
loved that girl, and when she first nestled her head on,
my shoulder, I said ‘ This is Heaven 1’ But after an hour
of the same position I began to get the cramps, and by
the time she had snoozled right up to my collar I felt as
if I would give a thousand pounds if she would only sit
up for a minute just to give me a chance to stretch my
arms.” Brutal—wasn’t if? But I should advise the-
fascinating creature who turns the scale at ten “stun”'
to paste this right in the centre of her looking-glass.
On dit that one of the most interesting things in this
interesting world is to see the amateur theatrical girl,,
whose fresco is an artist!.; triumph in the glare of the-
block, conic on to the stag a peifect guy with tomato-
cheeks and two layers of flour on her knowing little-
nose. Or else she pitifully asks somebody to do her face
for her—she really can’t manage it; “ you’ve no idea.
how unmanageable it is till you put it on, really ”—like-
wise the coarsely-" arranged ” eyebrows, and the blotched
lips. What a lot of science some women put into the
humbugging of their fellow-man !
The dual bill, a “Bed of Roses” and “ Elmine,” was
presented at .Sydney Protestant Hall on 3rd inst. by the
new "Irving” Dramatic Society for the benefit of their
coach, Mr. Fred B. Norton, who played Sir Guy, and was
the chief ray of hope in the mumbling, exasperating pro-
ceedings. Why amateurs act has never been discovered,
except they do it for the personal satisfaction they derive
of making a public exhibition of themselves. The new
Irvings made a two nights’ stand, playing “ Everybody's
Friend ” the preceding evening—and this budding amateur
tendency to create a bogus “ run ” to paper houses or
even empty benches is a great waste of time and energy.
In the case of the opera amateurs, who ran a week, they
hadn’t enough voice to sneeze with on the last night.
The final audit of the pupils of Herr and Madame
Kellermann at Sydney Y.M.C.A. on 2nd inst., was attended
by an immense audience—numbers being unable to fit in.
Mayor and Mrs. Burdekin presented the medals and the
prizes. Mrs. Burdekin wore heliotrope brocade and fine
white lace, and after the awards had been made, presented
Madame Kellermann with a gold lyre brooch set with
diamonds, from the pupils of the Conservatoire. Miss
Rose Masters and Miss Tompkins received the silver medal,
which had to be duplicated to meet the exigencies of an
unforeseen "dead heat,” and Miss Daisy Wildman and
Miss Leila Brown were certificate-takers on this occasion.
The latest decorative idea which has struck the girl who
has nothing to do but divert honest implements from the
path of duty, is to turn a warming-pan into a hall orna-
ment. It is polished all over till it shines like bronze,
after which the leather is bound about with straps of
parti-coloured ribbons like a maypole, which wind down
and fall in a shower over the brazen face of the “ subject.’
Then it is hung in the hal! with only one point in its
favour—namely, that it makes a very good gong.
Curly Potter and Brown Bellew finally farewelled
Sydney to an overflow ing sit-on-the-steps sort of house
on sth inst., when “ La Tosca” was the flourish. Bouquets
were plentiful—the nicest tribute being a basket of gar-
denias—and the wandering stars were recalled again and
again before Sydney allowed them to leave for the New'-
castle boat. But the deep-rooted conviction that nobody
but Kyrle or Cora would raise a furore in front led the
gifted brace to appear twice in answer to the tumult of a
house actually howling for Sass and only Sass. Which
was an appealing little touch of star human nature to
finish up with, wasn’t it ?
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

Editor Bulletin. —Sir,—As you have
noticed my article in the United Service Maga-
zine, I have to request that you will correct the
following—amongst several others—typographical
error. I did not write “ who displayed their
nationality by plundering the Chinese shops on all
occasions,” but “on one occasion.” This correc-
tion will save, no doubt, a great deal of natural,
but misplaced, indignation.—Yours faithfully, H.
R. Nicholls, Hobart.
A working-man of advanced views, whom
we have every reason to consider an honest, sober,
and industrious man, as well as one possessed of
an unusual share of intellect, writes to The Bul-
letin that he has only earned £2 since March
last, though he has striven hard to obtain an
opening. He points out that he could act as time-
keeper, or cargo-tallyer, or in any kind of work
where bodily strength was not so much a deside-
ratum as discernment, precision, and punctuality.
We shall be glad to hear of work to suit him.
The conscientious Chief Justice Parley
doesn’t think that Mr. Want’s Sunday Bill
(N.S.W.) will have an un-Christianising effect
on the day of rest, and thinks such a measure
necessary. The present N.S.W. Chief Justice is
a queer mixture and a most uncertain personage.
He hails from the north of Ireland, and looks
like a Roman father. The Evangelicals, to whom
he has given so nasty a back-hander, must be
paralysed to find that he is as liberal on the
Sunday question as a Holy Roman father.
A Bathurst correspondent points out that
in a recent horse-stealing case, Regina v. Booth,
tried at that town by Mr. Justice Foster, the jury
were unable to agree, and, after repeated ques-
tionings by his Honor, were finally locked up for
the night. Before locking them up, however, the
judge, as reported by a local paper, said that
under the circumstances it would be politic for the
minority to respect the opinion of the majority, for
whilst they might hold conscientious opinions,
they might have erred in their judgment Our
correspondent rises to inquire whether this is trial
by jury ?
Things were lively on the Trangie (N.S.W.)
railway platform a few mornings ago, when Ryrie,
M.P., and Commissioner Eddy exchanged civili-
ties. “You are the most insulting man I have
ever met in New South Wales,” exclaimed the
Railway Czar. “And you,” retorted Ryrie,
“are the most neglectful.” Ryrie had good cause
to boil over, for 1000 bale s of wool were lying in
the Weemabah shed and no trucks could be found
to cart them away. That was not all, however,
for 30 wool-laden trucks were actually stuck up at
Trangie simply because the railway officials could
not find any ropes to tie the bales on.
“ Captain Arthur Moore,” says the Hawk,
■“who was last employed in the Medea, is going
to Australia on what will probably prove to be a
fool’s errand. Lately the colonists consented very
grudgingly to partly maintain a small local
squadron, but they made it plain that, in the first
place, no foe was expected at Sydney or Mel-
bourne ; and, secondly, that if the mother-country
thought differently, it was open to her to provide
defences. General Edwards, who has recently
returned from an official visit to Australia,
reported in effect that the country might be
•captured by anybody who cared to take the
trouble, but the Colonial Defence Committee,
which comprises some of the leading officials
at the Horse Guards, pooh-pooh that report; and
Captain Moore has been sent out in the hope of
concerting with the colonists a less sensational
scheme than would satisfy General Edwards. The
Australian policy is a thoroughly selfish one.
They know that England will not part with their
country, and will, if necessary, protect it at her
own cost ; and this being so, they decline to do
more than pay the trifle which they have already
■engaged to do. Let Captain Moore’s mission be
called what it may, it really is an attempt to get
the colonists to contribute more, and he is bound
to fail.”

Despite its cleverness of book and sensa-
tionalism of scene, one is tempted now to think that
Jones and Herman were slightly overpaid when
they whacked the enormous sum they are said to
have received as their share of the profits of the
“ Silver King.” Staged as a stop-gap, .after four
rehearsals, it is fairly well played, all round, at
Sydney Royal, though it won’t stand comparison
with- the production of nearly six years past.
Laurence Oautley, who very much resembles a
Sydney pressman (when he wears a beard) who
once acted as secretary to G. 0. Miln, has a fine
voice and a good stage presence, and though
on Saturday night, he had to give most of his
attention to his lines, and could spare little for
“business,” yet gave a bright, manly and
pathetic reading that fetched tiie audience from
the first. But we don’t like his simulation of
drunkenness by walking as if his feet were
deformed. A voyage round the world hasn’t
toned down Greville’s fine owld Oirish accent, and
his Jaikes, creditable under the circumstances,
wasn’t improved by the introduction of stale
gags. Edward Sass’s “Spider ” is all right, and
Hans Phillips repeated his former success as
Corkett. Edwin Palmer made the fence, Coombe,
a Whitechapel Jew, but his performance was,
nevertheless, satisfactory. Nothing very good or
bad can be said about any of the other per-
formers, and the “Silver King” will be deposed
in favour of Toole on Thursday next (18th), when
the weather will be fine but frosty.
& * * *
On the last night of “She Stoops,” thegallery
of Sydney Royal was occupied by the Bank
Clerks’ Association and some friends. The young
money-changers kept fairly quiet during the show,
but sang lustily between the acts to the accompani
ment of their own band, and sent down a wreathed
basket of flowers to Mrs. Potter, who smiled on
them as widely as the proscenium would allow.
How is this sort of thing worked up, Tor this
theatre party was not by any means a spontaneous
tribute to the powers of a great actress ? On the
closing night of the season, accoi’ding to the S.M.
Herald, when the curtain fell on “La Tosca,”
La Potter and Bellew were “ called out no less
than six times.” As a matter of fact, they only
received one legitimate call, the others were un-
mistakeably for Sass, and they kept bowing their
acknowledgments to an audience that were deter-
mined to have him out, and would have afforded
the “ stars ” a show to take fifty - calls had they not
been eventually silenced by that actor’s appear-
ance. They have worked this little game success-
fully before, once with Edward Sass in “ Camille ”
at the end of the “gold” act, and once with
“Bob” Inman when he played Mercutio.

The two first acts of “ The Merry Wives
of Windsor ” (Rignold’s version) have been welded
together by using the “cradle ” to haul on and off
an interior that intervenes between two big
“ sets,” and now the curtain falls finally at 10.30.

indecencies of comic opera or of an allegedly

the play is too, too, you know.” Poor, benighted
beings ! “ The Wives ” will run till Boxing Night

The second week’s “biz” with “Dr. Bill”
has panned out even more profitably than the
first, and Manager Magee nightly hangs out
“ House Full ” on the Criterion’s portals as
proudly as a Soudan goat-slayer pins on his
nickel-silver medal. Judging by these signs, the
merry medico could continue to prescribe for the
laughter-loving for at least another month, bui
will be removed at Christmas, when a swap will
be done with the Bijou, and the classical half of
B. and B.’s comedy company will open here in
“Impulse,” while the farcical crowd paralyse
Melbourne folks with “ Dandy Dick,” who will
be closely followed by “ Dr. Bill.”

Following as usual in the wake of Henry
Irving and Ellen Terry, Cora Potter and
Kyrle Bellew have_ started out to give drama-
tic recitals. Notwithstanding the fair Cora’s
prediction that as soon as they got rid
of “ that horrid company ” things would go
smoothly, their opening night at Newcastle
was a failure. Bellew’s opening speech was
guyed and a series of monotonous recitations so
palled on the mining appetite that Mrs. P.’s
announcement that those who did not like the
show could have their money at the doors, caused
a regular stampede. But Manager Twinning’s
motto was “hand bag” not “hand back,” and so
the malcontents returned to take the value of their
coin out in interruptions.

The members of Coogoe Aquarium Club
spent a pleasant evening there one hot night last
week. Swimmer Corbett takes a benefit on
Saturday afternoon, when Professors Seguy and
Schuch meet with the foils, and Seguy on skates
encounters with singlesticks an amateur on foot.
An aquatic carnival also takes place in the baths,
when all the champions compete, and some feats
will be performed under water by Messrs Walker,
Parkinson, and Corbett.

Artistically, the Deslouis’ concert, at the
Y.M.O.A. Hall, was a great success. M. Deslouis
has a grand baritone voice, as most people know ;
the new soprana, Mile. Bestel, owing to nervous-
ness, did not do herself justice, and Van Ipereh,
the Dutch violinist, though not a Sarasate, gave
evidence of great ability. Mme. Gharbonnet-
Kellermarm and M. Kowalski also played, once
in concert.

“ Ld Fille de Madame Angofc,” being new
to half the population of Melbourne, crowded the
Opera House on Saturday nighty Time Was when
this most Parisian of operas reigned supreme ifi
its native country, and enjoyed the unique dis-
tinction of appearing at two London theatres
concurrently. But that was in the_ pre-Gilbert
and Sullivan period, when a little bright melody
Could earry a weak libretto, and the dullest of
gags passed fot a flash of genius. Modern audi-
ences have been educated to look for either smart
humour, keen satire, or, at least, a pleasant story
in comic opera productions, and so “ Madams
Angot ” goes slowly enough until the last act,
when the rival heroines invite one another to
come on and see which is the better cat, and the
curtains falls upon a general condition of moral
shadiness. If a better translation of the
original French libretto were given, it would
doubtless be found too Zolaesque for this pure
hemisphere, which has always taken kindly to
the aforesaid quatrel-scene, however. The salva-
tion of Saturday’s entertainment was a bouquet,
thrown bii to the strtge to be realistically fought
for by Nellie Stewart and Fanny Liddiard, who
tore the vegetables to pieces amidst thunders of
applause, and sent the audience home in a com-
paratively excited state. Otherwise no part of
the first performance, save only the Conspirators
Chorus, went as well as it will be going when this
appears in print, and perhaps the present rate of
“go ” isn’t sufficiently high to make* Angot a
teal successs The show seems to be clogged up
with music in which the principals are not at
honie. They sing trivial liiles instead of talking
them, and the awful necessity of catching Con-
ductor Plumpton’s wink as the signal to go fishing
for a note, seemed to oppress several souls on
Saturday. The whole of tho original music was
given for the first time irt Australia* to
advts. Some of it might slide back into obscurity
Without getting missed. Nellie Stewart makes
the most of the name part, While Fanny Lldaiarcl
plays Mdlle. Lange with a heap of dignity until
her blood gets up, and the fair pnma-donna
takes the starch out of her. Wood field,
voice fades apace, is likewise a free and
easy sort of Ange but regarded
as a Reactionary poet he doesn’t go far, judging
from the seditious song that Clairette sings.
When Larivaudiere offers Pitou some £ISOO tor
another song like that, he should take the money
and set up in the marine-store line, or something.
Snazelle, in the entirely heavy part of Larivau-
diere, is always artistic, but especially in the first
act. The others are about right, even young Jules
Simonsen being partially qualified to represent
Pomponnet, a man whose mission in life is to get
pushed over and requested to clear out._ Besides,
Bimonsen’s tenor is very, very sweet m a small
way. Two or three scenes are handsome, the
opera’s reputation for effective dresses is quite
maintained, and the chorus ladies stand around
rind do nothing ill particular with all imaginable
uccess.

Other Melbourne theatres are just putting
in their time quietly until Christmas. Large

** * £
The hypnotic drama died a gruesome death
at the Alexandra, its end being probably hastened
by a couple of alleged funny characters. On
Saturday night they filled up the gap left by the
late unlamented with “Monte Christo, a large,
imposing romance which flourished exceedingly
last season. The Count is one of Dampier s best
characters, and in it ho sticks to a fell purpose like
a majestic limpet until everybody but himself and
girl is either dead or awful near it. One weak
spot in the story is the old Abbe Somebody, who
doesn’t creak in his joints like Fred. Leslie used
to, and takes, moreover, a bit too long to die.
* Hi * *
“The Jilt” has not proved a big bonanza
at the Bijou. It is a bright comedy indeed, but
too impudently idiotic, in one 6r_ two places, tor a
sporting community. Besides, it has three long
waits between acts. The Bijou orchestra is far in
front of the average lot of fiddlers engaged to
produce slow music when anybody is dying or
making love (you find the same strains played in
either case), but people don’t go to see play in
Order to hear an orchestra, and the Bijom waits
are getting a bad name out of doors. On Satur-
day they revive “ The Squire ” for six nights only.

when that noble institution dies—supposing ,he
eandie—and St. Peter says, “Who are you? it
is ten to one that Riggs will fire off sixteen
revolvers at the cherubim and shout, Iracey the
Detective!”
** * *
Nothing else in the show-line is doing
better than tho Fasting Man at the Waxworks.
Up to last week Felix Tanner was looking bronzed
and happy, and it is evident that ho will want a
quantity of killing. He likes the business, ac-
cording to his own account, and has once fasted
a» long as twenty one days for the mere love of
not having anything to eat. Life at the Wax-
works suits him, except on Sundays, when
things are very quiet. It is an old belief that
wax figures go on the spree after closing
time, and get so drunk amongst them-
selves that the Royal Family turn into
the Chamber of Horrors by mistake, and Madame
Rachel substitutes herself for our Sovereign Lady.
But Tanner, finding them ghastly fixtures at
Kreitmayer’s place, complains that the pertinacity
of their Sunday glare makes him feel bad. Mean-
while, the ladies have taken this interesting young
man very much to heart. They load up his table
with bouquets from the florists, and gaze upon
him from afar off, remarking that they would give
anything in the world to be allowed to feed him
with a spoon, poor dear, Woman’s love is an
eccentric sentiment entirely,
** * *
In certain jiarts of Germany, according to
the boast of a Berlin paper, instead of heaving
bouquets at a favourite actress they throw useful
and sensible articles that will come in handy in
after life. There is a good deal to be said for this
practice. Flowers are beautiful enough for the
moment, tho’ next morning they are faded and
gone, but when the house rises in a burst of
enthusiasm and throws a bedstead and a quantity
of bedding, six chairs, a dining-table, a chest of
drawers, and a large quantity of underclothing to
the idol of the day it is a great deal more to the
point, and if it isn’t quite so beautiful that doesn’t
matter much.
** * *
The Adelaide Royal, after being closed for
a few nights, was taken possession of by a com-
pany of amateurs who produced for the first time
a comic opera called “ Sylvia,” the joint effort of
two local gentlemen. The amateurs acquitted
themselves fairly well, and were well patronised.
The music of the opera, which is the composition
of Cecil J. Sharp. 8.A., is tuneful and bright, but
the libretto by Guy Boothby is certainly not up
to much. Mr. Sharp takes the score to London
and expects to part with it there. The Phono-
graph has done very well in Adelaide, and the
Oyclorama with its picture of “Jerusalem at the
time of the Crucifixion ” has, so far, been an
enormous success.
A TOUGH QUESTION.
MISS GEORGINA MATSON.
MR. PAUL KITZ.
Of course this maid, she has attained majority,
Can wed without her guardian’s authority ;
But if she does—why, then they can deprive her—
So runs the will—of every blessed stiver.
And so the stricken dear must needs go weep,
For if her money she prefer to keep
(Her’s is indeed a melancholy case),
Her lover she for ever must deplace.
But if, on t’other hand, she should decide,
In spite of fate, to be her Kite's bride,
Her guardians’ opposition she can euchre
By giving up to them her filthy lucre.
A very nice dilemma. Will she spurn
The man for whom her very soul doth yearn ?
Or will she chance those fear-inspiring tits
With which her guardians tax the loving Kitz ?
Poor, persecuted fair, what will she do?
What line of conduct will she now pursue?
Her choice (and let us hope it won’t be rash)
Is limited to either love or cash.
Poor girl! poor girl! it is a dreadful strait,
Her final choice we anxiously await.
Which will it be ? Tis difficult to say—
Will love or filthy lucre win the day ?

twelve marks to drink four quarts of beer and eat eight

The gentleman “newspaperally ” described as “the
late eccentric Duke of Brunswick” was the hero of the
drollest of murders. He courted Madame Methfessel,
wife of a complaisant comedian, but Cornet, another
actor, objected, so while Brunswick was embracing
Madame on the stage one night Cornet got the prompter,
in a joke, to ring up the curtain, displaying the tableau
to an excited audience, whereupon Brunswick drew his
sword and gave the prompter a death stab.

Old Tom Phool, one of whose favourite jokes is to give
a cabman a penny and then apologise when stuck-up,
protesting he thought it half-a-crown, having propor-
tionately paid a Napier (N.Z.) cabman for a day’s riding,
was summoned and compelled to pay full fare and costs.
When he left Napier the cabbies rolled up and hooted him
out of the town.

questions concerning the safety of Sydney Opera House
and the conditions of its license. We offer no opinion as

skin on the result. Meantime the Premier has ordered
an inquiry.

Cleopatra Bernhardt is about to marry, it is said, Antony

Myra Kemble purchased Fred Broughton’s “Peer of
the Realm,” which has not yet been played anywhere as
well as “ Or. Bill.” She would have brought along “ Aunt
Jack,” the play Mrs. John Wood scored so heavily in, had
not the sum asked for the Australian rights (£1000)
seemed prohibitive. She was delighted with Sarah
Bernhardt, whom she declares to be “the only actress in
the world,” but was not impressed with the abilities of
her support. The Rosalind of Ada Rehan was to her a
revelation ; she had never seen the part properly played
before. Irving and Ellen Terry impressed her very
highly, but, like all other British “stars,” they are sup-
ported, outside the principal characters, by a lot of
mumbling, lisping, ambling inanities who have stepped
from the drawing-room on to the stage, and who simply
walk in a dreary, haw-haw, doncherknow, tired-to-death
manner through their parts. Miss Kemfile considers
Leonard Boyne, now playing in “An English Rose,” the
brightest juvenile on the British stage, and imagines that
William Archer is the most honest, outspoken, arid fear
less among the critics, though Clement Scott speaks very
bitterly of the modern “ cup and saucer” style of acting.
The first act of “ Man and Woman,” the play of Robert
Buchanan’s, in which the fair Myra played the lead, is
very strong, but the second resolved itself into a duologue
between a fossilised vicar and a chirpy curate as to the
indissolubility of the marriage-contract, and that damned
the show. Robert Buchanan is generally looked on as
one skilful at hashing-up dainty theatrical dishes, but who
cannot evolve anything substantial and yet tasteful from
his own inner consciousness. Edouin offered her the part
in “ Our Flat,” vacated at the Strand by the secession of
Fanny Brough to Drury Lane, but she refused it,
thinking the play likely to be a financial failure. She also
refused, owing to ill-health, an offer to play the adven-
turess (played by Alice Lingard) in “A Million of Money ”
now running at the latter theatre.
Myra Kemble wouldn’t advise any of the perfesh to go
to Britain. She says that lots of capable and clever
people there are only on salary three months in the year.

boy, a travelling of the Post-office, a pantaloon
at Covent Garden, cutter-out to a theatrical costumier,
