The “ Cleopatra ” of Sardou and Moreau differs widely
from that of Shakespeare, in that it has fewer scenes.
Cleopatra makes her first entry in a gilded barge that
floats along the Cydnus heralded by harp and song, and
the first curtain falls on Antony joining her therein/ The
next scene, laid in the palace at Raineses (a magnificent
piece of stage-work), shows Antony in the toils. The
third scene, that of the Palace of Memphis, with the pano-
ramic view of which The Bulletin has already spoken, is
finest of all. There Cleopatra hears of the marriage of
Antony to Octavia. The fourth act shows Antony’s
palace at Actium on the eve of the naval engagement,
and his jealousy of Cleopatra and Kephren. The con-
cluding scene is laid in the Queen’s gardens. Antony
staggers in stabbed, to die, and Cleopatra, putting an asp
on her bosom, expires hanging on his lips. Bernhardt’s
dresses are not dresses, simply “drapery so artfully
pinned about her slender form as to bring out the har-
monious lines of the body in their most perfect charm.”

judge in one of the divisions of Sydney Supreme
Court, his loud whoop penetrates to the other

Mr. J. H. Rainford, a new man, has just,
been elected to Sydney City Corporation by a.
substantial majority. Mr. Rainford promises the
electors a variety of things, and The Bulletin
will be inclined to judge him by the action he
adopts with respect to demanding full public
enquiry into the case of the Sewerage Board
official who is alleged to have been dismissed the
service for no other cause than that he treated the
property of Millionaire Mayor Burdekin as though
it belonged to somebody who was not a gilded
civic magnate. If the statements made by the
dismissed official, Macdonnell, are true —and the
latter has shown us testimonials as to character
which entitle to respectful attention any grave
complaint made by him—he is the victim of an
inexpressibly disgraceful piece of gratuitous
tyranny. The Bulletin holds no stock in Mr.
Macdonnell, but it asks the independent members
of the City Corporation to see that he is given
a fair trial on the charge upon which he is to be
deprived of his living.

Why Graveson Died.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
It was six o’clock on Sunday, 19th Decem-
ber, and the strong Barrier sun beat in at the
window of a bedroom in the Bromide Hotel,
Felspar-street, Argent-town. The rays fell on
two variously-shaped but generously-proportioned
figures broadly outlined by the sole covering of the
thin sheets made necessary by the molten atmos-
phere. The owners of these figures, filling two
small bunks placed one on each side of the window,
were the sub-editor and the mining-reporter of the
leading local daily. Sunday was their only “lie-
abed” morning, and they were enjoying it to the
full. One bed was short and stout —the other long
and spare. The sub-editor, a dumpy fat man all
arches and curves, had installed himself in the
long bed, and his broad body overhung the edge
of the mattrass and cast a shadow on the. floor.
As for the long reporter his attenuated legs pro-
jected fully 18 inches over the foot of the bunk
naturally intended for his chief.
They were having the usual Sunday consulta-
tion, the stereotyped subject being the proposition
of new methods for “ raising the wind.”
The sub-editor extended a hairy paw from under
the sheet and pushed along the floor towards the
mining scribe a tray on which stood two glasses—
empty;
“ Teddy,” said he, “go to the door and call the
girl for more whisky.”
“I can’t, really, old man,” answered the littera-
teur. “ I had Lord knows how much trouble get-
ting to bed last night without seeing the Gentle
Publican. He was on the war-path for me with
his bill.”
“ Oh, don’t mind a little matter like that. Thai
gay nonchalant air of yours will let you stand off
for a month yet.”
“Nonchalant air be hanged,” said the other,
modestly. “It won’t work, I tell you. You get
the drinks and I’ll sub. for you two nights next
week. I’ll square Billy. ’
(For the information of the uninitiated, it may
be stated that “ Billy ” was the editor.)
The sub-editor was unable to withstand the
allurement of two nights off. He got out of bed,
picked up the tray and glasses, and opened the
door. He cautiously shielded his unclad limbs
behind the panels, extended into the passage the
hand grasping the tray, like a hairy semaphore
stretched to the youthful drinker on the road to the
devil, and yelled, “ Maria—Jane —Sarah—what-
ever your name is—drinks ! ”
A herculean Irish girl answered the summons
and disappeared with the tray in the direction of
the bar. She returned a few moments later and
delivered two glasses of whisky to the dumpy
pressman who, nodding significantly, said, “In the
ledger,” and then vanished.
“Here’s luck, Teddy,” said the sub.
“Same to you, Charley,” echoed the reporter.
But by a strong effort of will he arrested the pro-
gress of the spirit, and said “Listen !” They
listened attentively, and heard the voice of the
landlord, who was bullying the Dutch-built Irish
domestic.
“What for do ye be lettin’ thim drunkin paper
min have more dhrinks on the nod for?” he asked.
“Bad luck to thim, they niver has any money,
and whin they’re not sphendin’ it, they do be
dhrinkin’ wid th’ shlate.”
“Here’s luck, Teddy,” said the sub.
“Same to you, Charlie,” echoed the reporter’.
And this time the whisky had an uninterrupted
flow.
The sub-editor broke the silence ensuing.
“Whom shall we stand off next ?” he enquired.
“ Don’t know —we’ve worked ’em all, ” responded
his henchman.
“ Um,” said the sub, and silence oecurrea
again.
Suddenly that staid and sober pressman (from
a pressman’s point of view), Mr. Edward Down-
thorne, jumped out of bed and executed an ex-
cellent imitation of the Dance of Death.
“ I’ve got it,” he said. “ Old Graveson.”
His room-mate gazed at him admiringly.
“ There’s not another man on the Barrier would
have thought of Graveson,” he commented ap-
preciatively. “Teddy, I always thought you
were a genius—now I’m sure of it.”
“ It’s as good as done,” continued Teddy. “ To-
morrow we’ll fall on him with the sword of the
Lord and of Gideon.”
They dozed again. The sub. was full of the
project. He sat up in bed and called to his
friend, “ Are you sure we can fix it ?”
But the literary and financial genius was
snoring.
* * #
Old Graveson was not old—only middle-
aged. He had arrived on the field some months
prior to the date of this incident, bringing with
him about £3OOO in cash, the purchase-money of a
livery-stable business, and his wife, a shrewish
young woman, who acted as clerk for her husband
and who took charge of the income and paid the
bills. Graveson had been rather “chummy”
with the two pressmen and they now deter-
mined to put his friendship to the test. But they
were ignorant of the existence of two important
facts —one was that Graveson was infernally mean
and the other that his wife was the cashier.
On Monday afternoon the sub-editor and the
reporter walked over to Graveson’s stables in
Beryl-street. They told a groom that they wished
to see Mr. Graveson and were ushered into a
dusty little office. About five minutes elapsed
and —Mrs. Graveson entered. This was some-
thing the borrowing ones had not anticipated.
Mrs. Graveson knew the pressmen by sight, as,
indeed, did everybody else. Most of the dwellers
on the field and its environs were steady, uncom-
promising South Australians—half Methodist—
half trader. They occasionally placed their
Methodism outside on the mat when it paid them
to do so, but they hung on to the more substantial
god of the Coinage with all their might and never
relaxed their hold. Consequently the pressmen
who had been imported from Sydney filled them
with supreme contempt and joy. Surprise and
contempt because the newspaper men indulged in
gorgeous “ bursts ” on Saturdays and drove buggies
on Sundays, and were penniless for the rest of the
week. Joy because they spent all the money they
could lay their hands on and were consequently
good for trade. So Mrs. Graveson knew her
visitors quite as well as the occasion required.
“We wished to see Mr. Graveson, madam,”
said the graceless reporter.
Mrs. Graveson was very sorry—her husband
would not be in town for two days.
“Never mind, then,” interjected the sub.,
“ we’ll call again.”
“I transact all my husband’s business,” said the
lady, whose ruling passion was curiosity.
The conspirators glanced uneasily at one
another.
“We really wouldn’t like to bother you,” re-
turned Charlie. “ It’s of no consequence. Is it,
Ted ? ”
“None whatever—the most immaterial business
in the world.”
“If it is so trivial, then, ’ insisted Mrs. Grave-
son, “ I can attend to it at once ; can’t I ? ”
The visitors thought that she couldn’t or
wouldn’t do anything of the kind, but they kept
the opinion to themselves. This inquisitive
woman would have to be stopped somehow.
An inspiration came to Downthorne. “ Fact
is, madam,” he said, in a confidential tone,
“ Our business is of a very delicate nature and
concerns Mr. Graveson only. To him alone must
our story be told. That’s so, isn’t it, Charlie?”
The sub-editor took his cue immediately.
“Quite correct, Mrs. Graves n. It is an
extremely delicate matter, and we could not think
of treating with a lady.” And here he looked as
'f the very idea of broaching the subject would
“FORGETTING HIMSELF.”
YOUNG WIEE: “The woman that nursed me when I was a baby called to-day.”
Old Husband (married the third time) : “Oh, is she still alive?”
wither him with shame.
“Is it so very private, then, gentlemen ?”
“It is, madam,” said the sub. “It is unfor-
tunate —very unfortunate—that we should have
missed Mr. Graveson to-day. But we members
of the press must do our duty. We cannot treat
with you, but as soon as Mr. Graveson returns I
hope the thing will be fixed for good.”
The two journalists bowed and walked out of
the office, leaving the inquisitive woman racked
with the pangs of unsatisfied curiosity.
# # #
That afternoon the editor of the afternoon
daily came into the office of the morning paper.
Downthorne told him of the visit to Graveson’s
office : “ We’re going to tap him when he comes
back,” he concluded.
The editor of the evening journal shook his head
sadly. “No good,” he said. “I’ve tried him my-
self. He’s as mean as his wife is jealous and in-
quisitive.”
“ Inquisitive and jealous, is she?” said the sub.
slowly.
# # *
On the following Friday Graveson sought
his wife’s visitors of the preceding Monday. He
button-holed Downthorne and unfolded a tale of
ill-usage.
“What was that business—that secret?” he
asked anxiously. “My wife says you have a
secret of mine. She wants me to tell her what it
is, and I can’t because I have no secret and she
thinks I can and won’t.”
Teddy answered with great dignity : “Sir, this
pretended ignorance will not suit me. I decline
to speak to you again or deem you exonerated
from any blame until I see an apology in all the
Barrier newspapers to the lady you have so grossly
insulted.”
Then he walked off, leaving Graveson gasping
for air. By and bye the livery-stable‘keeper re-
covered sufficiently to seek the sub-editor.
“ What does it all mean?” he asked the dumjjy
journalist. “What lady did I insult, and whom
am I to apologise to ? ”
“ Gravey,” said Charles, looking at him admir-
ingly, “You’re a born actor—you do it beauti-
fully. Whom did you insult ? Go off, you sly old
’possum, go off,” and the penniless pressman dug
Graveson in the ribs—the great Graveson, who
could have bought up half the journals and jour-
nalists in the country —for the Barrier had been
good to him.
The livery-stable-keeper could get no informa-
tion from anybody, and he went home no wiser
thair he had left it. He was foolish enough to tell his
wife that afternoon that he had been accused of
insulting some lady unknown—to him at any rate.
This, of course, aggravated Mrs. Graveson’s
curiosity. It did more. It inflamed her jealousy.
Henceforth Graveson’s life would be a misery to
him, she said. And she kept her promise to the
letter.
# * #
The newspaper-men rubbed on well enough
without Graveson’s money. They had instituted
the advance system in the office now, and regu-
larly mortgaged their Saturday’s salary on the
preceding Tuesday.
On June 11 last Graveson departed this life.
The medical man’s belief was that death had
resulted from a general decline of the vital powers.
But two journalists, now leading respectable, sys-
tematic lives, feel that the stain of the dead man’s
blood rests upon their souls. They know that
Mrs. Graveson worried her husband into his coffin
because he would not tell her the secret which was
not. Randolph Bedford.
Haytian proverb: “Never insult the
crocodile’s mother until you have safely forded
the river !”
Professor N. B. Webster gives the palm
to milk as a muscle-former, but recommends un-
fermented grape-juice as a heat-producing, fat-
forming food.
A Maoriland citizen has put his foot down
with sudden vigour on the electoral canvasser —a
nuisance that is in full bloom in the land of the
bulgy moa. He was digging in the back garden
when his wife informed him that a most respect-
able gentleman wanted to see him, and as he
wore an old pair of pants that were gone at the
knees, and a torn, slimy coat, and a broken hat,
he had to hurry inside and get into his clothes in
a surreptitious, underhand way before he could
confront the visitor. Also he had to wash himself
and hunt up a clean collar, and shine his boots,
and then when he went into the parlour he found
nobody there except an oily character who asked
him if he would vote for Smith. He pointed out
that Smith was a man of genius and a friend to
industry, and that Smith loved to grasp the
horny hand of the working man, and then he went
on to say that as a supporter of the great cause of
toil . At this point, however, he was put
under the pump by the choleric settler, and when
he broke loose and fled he looked like an escaped
cataract. And when he paused in his flight
to see what had become of his hat he discovered
that the infuriated voter was jumping on it.
Sammy Jones, the American evangelist,
recently enquired in church: “Is there a man
present who never spoke a cross word to his
wife?” The silence was oppressive; no one
moved till a round, good-natured looking man got
up. “Thank God!” exclaimed Sam, “there is
one man who never spoke a cross word to his
wife !” The good-natured man smiled a fat, bland
smile and said : “No, air, I never did. J’m a
bachelor.” Then he put on his hat and calmly
walked out of the door.
A. Tuscan poet has just published a small
volume of poems, written, he asserts, in every case
by the fitful light of errant fireflies. Swinburne
declared that he performed a similar feat in the
case of one poem, and Gerald Massey, not to be
outdone, wrote a poem by the light of a glow-worm
that crawled slowly over the paper. But there’s
nothing in all that! The London correspondent
of a Sydney evening paper writes his spicy
divorce-case notes by the phosphorescent glimmer
of a well-kept cod’s head, and our best bush poet
can see to shave himself by the glow of his own
grog-blossoms.
Woman.
Eight hours work, eight hours sleep, eight hours
play, that is the cry of the mechanic of to-day.
We however, have no sympathy with such cries,
we do not believe in men who want to work but
eight hours and keep their butchers, bakers,
grocers, chemists and even wives at work from
twelve to sixteen hours daily. Wives’ lives now-
a-days are too frequently lives of drudgery. Up
at 5 a.m. to get her husband’s breakfast, then
children to wash and despatch to school, then
dinner to prepare, and so on to ten at night, one
continual round of work, wash and worry. Is it
any wonder that they are prone to early decay
and premature physical decline ? It is to be
expected that they would be specially susceptible
to anaemia, indigestion, liver complaint and head-
aches. Their mode of life and long hours indoors
is almost sure to induce such diseases. On account
of the duties nature has imposed upon the fair
sex, great care should be taken of their physical
health; on the first approach of weakness or
debility of any kind, a course of Clements Tonic
should be taken. Eor female complaints Clements
Tonic is a specific, it regulates the several func-
tions of the organs of importance, it strengthens
the blood, nerves, heart and brain. It fortifies
the system against disease by enriching the blood
with its natural constituents to repair the waste
and loss sustained through the various changes of
condition the female life experiences. Clements
Tonic should be taken at all critical times ; as it
contains the material essential to the female
physical organism, it must be effective. Of this
there is no doubt: Mrs. E. C. McCarthy, West
Maitland, N.S.W., writes : —August 19th, 1889.
Dear Mr. Clements, —I address you thus familiarly
because I consider you a brother and a friend,
although I never met you. I have suffered ex-
cruciating agony from nervous toothache for years,
on and off, and could not get relief. 1 had
seven teeth extracted (what a wrench those
dentists give us), one after the other, but as soon
as one was out the pain was passed to another. I
was given one of your Medical Guides, and there
read your theory that toothache is often a
symptom of Anaemia, and I had other signs which
I found you mentioned as being the effects of
anaemic conditions. I also read your boast of the
wonderful effects of Clements Tonic; I was scepti-
cal, smiled and disbelieved, but thought a trial
would do no harm, so sent for some, and in two
days—Oh ! joy, the pain was gone, I continued
the Tonic, and am cured at last; I never expected
to be, but am grateful enough to you, and you
may use this if worth your while. And Mrst
Whalley describes her case in these words
Wagga Wagga, N.S.W., Jan. 12, 1889. Your
wonderful Tonic has done me more good than
years of doctor’s treatment, and has restored me
to perfect health. Eor years I suffered from
nervous debility and its usual string of accompany-
ing ailments; sometimes I would be despondent
and melancholy, at others would have torturing
neuralgia, or nervous headaches; sometimes 1
would be quite worn out, tired and weak, and
unable to work. I awoke in the morning with
tired, aching limbs, swollen feeling in the eyes,
as if they would burst, sometimes headache
and frequent nausea and vomiting of mucous-like
green matter, and various unpleasant symptoms.
Although prejudiced against proprietary medi-
cines, I determined to try Clements Tonic and
Dr. Fletcher’s Pills, I took two pills every alterna-
tive night, and a tablespoonful of Clements Tonic
an hour before each meal-time, and in three days
I began to improve, and am now quite well. I
took seven large bottles of Tonic and two boxes
of pills ; my constipation is quite cured, and I
feel stronger and healthier than ever before.
Further evidence can be adduced to the above,
which we think is conclusive.

POINTS
Labour organisation in Queensland is less
than six years old.
Queensland Customs returns have dropped
£28,000 per month in three years.
The coal imported from Japan during the
late strike was hewn and worked by convict-
labour.
South Australia is going to construct a
railway to Innamincka, so as to annex Queens-
land’s pastoral trade.
Judge Boucaut emphatically denies any
intention to re-enter the political arena, adding
that he has no wish to pose as a Rip Van Winkle.
Are there two of the name in the N.S.W.
Legislative Assembly, that the member for
Cumberland should be always styled “Mr. Frank
Parnell V”
Mr. L. F. Heydon, M.L.C., has gone to
London per Orotava, probably to see if he cannot,
even at this late date, block the N.S.W. divorce-
hill.
Alderman Tomkinson —a perky little Tory
of Tories -was ignominiously rejected at Adelaide
• municipal elections for kicking against municipal
: rates being assessed on unimproved values.

j S.A. Legislative Council has rejected the
Free Education proposal of the Playford Ministry.
The voting was 12 to 11. Fancy tivelve men
being able to delay the wishes of a whole
; Province !
Premier Munro has balanced his Cabinet
with a trio of ex-officio Natives, not deemed smart
enough for portfolios, but too dangerous to leave
out, altogether a false position to put Natives, or
even Scotchmen, into.
London Spectator points out that the
inefficiency of the House of Commons is due to
. the faet that it contains too many representatives.
Yec, in proportion to population N.S.W. elects
five times as many members !
The first Separation meeting held at
Townsville (Q.) was “packed” with wharf-
lumpers, who were instructed to “ bust it up,” at
the instance of a present M.L. A., who is now one
of the leaders of the movement.
S. W. Brooks, ex-M.P., and F. Ransome,
organiser and secretary respectively of the Bris-
bane Employers’Union dub the secretaries of the
labour bodies “paid agitators.” The epithet seems
to reasonably apply both ways.
What a farce to leave the appointment of
Engineer-in-Chief for N.S. Wales Railways to the
late unsatisfactory Engineer-in-Chief, Mr. Whit-
ton, and his brother-in-law, Sir John Eowler!
Was there ever so brazen a proposal ?
The Review of Reviews, discussing the
Australian strike, speaks of “the working-men of
the colony of Melbourne.” Evidently Mr. Stead,
of the empire of London, near Bangor, is not such
a well-informed person as is generally supposed.
The scheme for local legislatures in
Queensland, which, in its way, is perhaps as
striking a bit of statecraft as has emanated from
any Australian Government, is the work of two
men. M‘llwraith supplied the ideas ; Griffith put
them into form.
The belief grows that Sir Charles Lilley,
C. J., will be off the Bench and into the Queens-
land Assembly at next general election. J udged
by his litterings and writings—although he has
accepted the prefix bauble —Lilley is in most
things radical to the back-bone.
To be a successful working-man in New
South Wales, according to Mr. Torpy, who was a
wine-and-spirit dealer and is now a newspaper-
proprietor, a man must be thrifty and sober, and
carefully avoid early marriage. The surest way,
however, is to drink lemonade, run a loan-office,
and never get married at all.
The two young Tory cranks who edit
London Whirlwind are busy advocating the
restoration of the Jacobite dynasty. They have
actually crowned a lady whom they style “ Mary
of Modena,” and lately sent to the printers a
diatribe in her favour styled “A Call to Arms,”
which the compositors refused to “set up,”and so
the paper didn’t come out.
The Sydney branch of the Irish National
League has decided by a majority of two votes
only to uphold Parnell. Bah ! In a year Parnell,
the hot-blooded Lothario, will be forgotten. In
five hundred years Parnell, the cold-blooded
statesman, will be remembered. The O’Shea
affair is simply the wolf’s pretext for eating the
lamb. Parnell must show that he is not a lamb.
Since Pilcher, Q. 0., ceased to represent
West Macquarie, where the powerful MThillany
interest —the Q.O. having wedded a daughter of
that house—always secured his return, the Webb-
cwm-Paul combination have been hard-pushed for
decent candidates. Once they got a copper-miner
in tow who promised to stand if ho were not asked
to speak. This was agreed to, the copper-man was
returned, and ho never once opened his lips while
in Parliament.
After all, who believes in Gladstone’s per-
fect sincerity over the Irish Question ? He
chopped round five years ago merely to take the
wind out of the sails of Salisbury and the Conser-
vatives, who were coquetting with Parnell, and
going to do a sort of Disraeli-dish-the-Whigs trick.
This opinion does not affect one’s ideas of the
rights and wrongs of the Hibernians, but only the
G.O.M. as such —i.e., Great Oily Gammony.
The Duke of Argyle does not believe in
Socialism and Land Nationalisation. Yet he said
recently : “ The absolute ownership of every man
in his own muscles and in his own brain seems to
be an axiomatic truth.” Socialists claim nothing
more ; but, they point out that the man who owns
only muscles and brains is compelled by circum-
stances to submit to the expropriation of others
better endowed with goods, and that his muscle
and brain virtually belong to those possessed of
land or capital.
People laugh, with reason, at the dis-
organised state of the N.S.W. Parliamentary
Opposition. However, they may shortly have
an opportunity to laugh, with just as much
reason, at the disintegrated condition of the
Government, for it is quite on the cards that
Premier Parkes will, in accordance with a rumor
lately current, retire to England next year, after
availing himself of the precedent established by
the grant of £lO,OOO to old Jack Robertson.
Says John Morley—and the statement is
interesting at the present juncture:—“ There are
half-a-dozen men among the Irish leaders at this
moment who, in aptitude for public business, in
readiness, in mental vigour, in fitness for all the

Parkes says that the shareholders in the
Broken Hill water company are wealthy men
whose interests would not be served by offering
bribes. Consequently they did not offer bribes.
On the other hand, if they had been poor men
they couldn’t have offered bribes. Consequently
they would not have done it in that case either.
In fact, neither section of humanity, according to
Parkes, ever offers bribes. Yet somebody does it
occasionally, though who in thunder it can be the
old man evidently forgot to explain.
Sir John Downer would have carried his
no-confidence motion against the Playford
Ministry had it not been for a split amongst the
Opposition. The motion in question shows how
peculiarly parties are constructed in S.A., and
how the desire for office is the prime consideration.
Sir John Downer is the most bigoted of Tories,
yet democrats, land - reformers and radicals
swarmed around him like flies directly there
seemed a chance of office. Had Downer’s reso-
lution been carried, the Playford Ministry would
have been the sixth wrecked since 1884 !
The N.S.W. Public Service Inquiry Com-
mission reports that the Superintendent and
assistant-superintendent of Telegraphs have not
been on friendly terms for a considerable period,
and recommends that both offices be abolished,
The same report recommends that all instruction
in telegraphy be given at Sydney Technical
College. Since the commission closed, the
telegraph-department instruction branch has been
reconstructed, at a cost of £lOOO, and instead of
abolishing the Telegraph Instructor’s sinecure
billet (there are no pupils to instruct), no less than
four “ Instructors ” now hold appointments. So
much for Royal Commission reports and sugges-
tions.
Personal Items.
The 29th inst. will be Gladstone’s 81st
birthday.
Stanley says that Moslemism decreases.
So does Stanleyism.
Henry Labouchere’s sister is wife of the
Bishop of Rochester.
Lewis Cohen, the ex-Fiji chief, has been
re-elected Mayor of Adelaide.
The oldest of N.S.W. clergymen, Rev.
Thomas Donkin, died at the Kurrajong, near
Richmond, on Nov. 28, aged 90.
Gov. Kintore, seeing the absurdity of the
thing, has obtained the release of the man who
was given a month for boohooing him.
On dit that C. H. Buzacott has resigned
the managing directorship of the Brisbane News-
paper Co. in favour of his son William.
Editor Armit, of Cooktown Courier, is
the man who “ captained ” the Argus New
Guinea expedition to anything but glory in 1883.
The Melbourne Southern Cross says that
General Booth has ground his dead wife’s bones
to make theatrical bread for the Salvation Army.
To give the devil his due, “Canterbury”
Hutchinson was the first man to introduce the
subject of local option to an Australian audience.
Alderman J. H. Rainford is the only pub-
lican in Sydney City Corporation. Time was
when half the aldermen was engaged in the liquor-
traffic.
Mr. C. L. Garland, M.L.A. (N.S.W.), has
just buried his grandmother. She was 90 years
of age, and had five children, whose descendants
now number 107.
In addition to his vice-regal screw and
“ perks,” Gov. Norman, of Queensland, enjoys £8
per day and £ll7s. expenses as an English general
of the sixth grade.
Charles Henry Elliston, the Hobart
barrister, is grandson of Elliston, the famous
Covent Garden and Drury Lane comedian, of the
early part of this century.
A Melbourne “ scalawag ” claims to be a
a son of Chief Justice Coleridge (England). As
he gets very drunk he has made out a sort of a
prima-facie title to the name he claims.
It is the pet boast of one of the proprie-
tors of the Brisbane Courier that he reads nothing
in that journal but the “ads.” Consequently his
reading has been rather limited of late.
Chief Justice Lilley’s elder brother is a
flourishing butcher in Newcastle-on-Tyne, Eng.
The Queensland Lilley, under certain modifica-
tions, has done a little in the same way.
A certain Northern Queensland member
is a modern Esau. Nature erroneously anticipated
that Jack would reside in Siberia, and therefore
provided him with a redundant coat of hair !
The late Mayor of Gayndah (Q.) is a
heathen Chinee. One of the wealthiest and most
respected citizens of Normanton (Q.) is also a
Celestial. Still the Chinese will have to go !
Under - secretary - for - agriculture Peter
M‘Lean is the “ Arch-Good-Templar ” of Queens-
land. He is also author of numerous tomes on
Darling Downs vintages, and how they may be
mellowed and improved.
Sir John Fowler, inspector of railway-
material for the N.S.W. Government, and Mr.
JohnWhitton, ex-engineer in chief for railways
in N.S.W., are brothers-in-law. Which accounts
for a lot of milk in the cocoa-nut.
Last week a lunatic with an open Bible
caused some commotion by chasing Premier
Parkes all the way up Macqiiarie-street, Sydney.
The Bulletin could guess in two tries on what
commandment the crank had his finger.
Christie Murray, on off-nights at Adelaide
Royal, may be seen in the rear row of the almost
empty dress-circle “scanning” the stage and
making mental notes of the play. He swears that
it is untrue he is going to write another book.
J. Brunton Stephens has become a fossil
bound in red-tape since entering the Queens-
land Civil Service, and has for long done no
literary work of any consequence. His only
regular “screed ” is a weekly column of milk-and-
watery comment in a Brisbane paper.
The English jiress gushes over good father
Moltke’s 'JOth biithday. Moltke is a great soldier,
but a dog all the same. He is a Dane by birth,
and yet helped to humble his own little fatherland
—than which the sun shines on ho more gallant
country.
Sir Barnes Peacock, Q.C., has died, aged
80. ThiS old gentleman has for many years
delivered most of the judgments of the Privy
Council. He was a particular friend of ex-Justice
Faucett, of N.S.W., whose judgments have been
treated with phenomenal tenderness by the Privy
Council.
Parnell has ceased to be an honorary citizen
of Edinburgh, for the moral Town Council of that
place rose to the occasion and threw him out.
Morality is the strong point of the average Scotch-
man, and it is probably owing to this that his
country about tops the European record for
illegitimacy.
“The Princess von Reuss, wife of the Gei'-
man Ambassador in Vienna, puts on a lot of
‘side.’ She was patronising the newly-intro-
duced wife of the U.S. Minister, when that lady
turned on her and said loudly : ‘ls that your
usual style of talk, or do you only put it on when
you go out ? ’”
H. A. Wood, Formerly S.A. Public
Trustee, and now in “retirement” in America,
was about the smartest fraud that ever struck
Adelaide. Just before his disappearance, he
obtained a costly opinion from a leading lawyer
as to whether a person could abscond with safety
to a certain part of America. The opinion was in
the affirmative. Three months afterwards Wood
went there, and the Government had subsequently
to pay the lawyer’s bill !

Society
Jersey’s aides who arrived by the Oratava
Are anything but lovely, especially Goshen, in
fact, they’re decidedly weedy. When the big
ship sighted their new land of Goschen they
shouted at least a hundred bottles of champagne
for the saloon passengers.
* * *
That interesting widower, John Hurley,
M.L. A. for Hartley, is about to get married to
the daughter of an old and respected Sydney
citizen. John still retains strong traces of the
manly beauty which, in the old days of Hill End,
made him Adonis-in-chief among 10,000 men.
# * *
“ Sir Arthur Nicholson, Bart.,” it is tele-
graphed, “ visits England shortly for an extended
period.” Well, supposing so ! Why should this
kind of telegram be inflicted on us ? What do we
care where he goes or how long he stays ? Sir
Arthur found himself with a hereditary title
and no money, and so he became a policeman—a
very gentlemanly policeman, indeed. Then he
married a Geelong girl with a heap of money, and
now he is going where they all go.
* * *
“Sir Joseph Lister, Bart., an eminent
surgeon, asserts that Dr. Koch has discovered a
simple and cheap remedy for two of the worst con-
tagious diseases, but that particulars will not be
disclosed for months.” When this cable appeared
a powerful syndicate was formed by the Sydney
quack doctors with a view to cornering the
secret. It now transpires that tetanus and diph-
theria are the maladies in question.

sacred land, for breach

not marry his charmer
becuise he was related
to Dean Alford and the
Bishop of Hongkong !
* # *
It is not often that
the ghastly, hidden mys-
teries of the Sydney
boarding - house are
dragged into the fierce,
white light of publicity.
The other day a Sydney
suburban resident unex-
pectedly gained know-
ledge of part of the
unhallowed rites. At
various intervals an old
man had called begging
for broken victuals, which
had been given to him.
One morning he ap
peared with a bag over
his shoulder, which bag
was evidently full. “Look
here, ma’am,” he said,
mysteriously, “you keeps a boarding-house,
don’t you?” “No, I don’t,” was the answer.
The man’s countenance fell. “Why do you
ask?” was the natural question, for he looked a
very unlikely candidate for board and lodging.
“ Well, it was just this way, you see. I thought,
of course, we could make a deal. I goes round
begging victuals all day, and then I disposes of it
to boarding-houses, and here’s a lot might have
suited you.” He removed the bag from his
shoulder and opened it. They were all there—
the hard, defiant chunk of corned beef, the greasy,
cold roast, the congealed soup-meat, the in-
destructible steak, the immortal cold bone of
mutton, the fried chop, the mysterious sausage,
the cold, waxen potato, and several substantial
nuggets of yesterday’s^bread.
* * *
Miss Sutherland-’s attack on the Women’s
Hospital, Melbourne, as a training-place for the
young Sawbonses, dabbling in common china
before touching porcelain, Dresden and Sevres
ware, brought up a furious reclame at next com-
mittee-meeting. All the committee are dames,
and Secretary Gibson holds his hen convention.
Miss Sutherland is a sturdy Democrat, of plain,
plodding style, while the convention is of the
Toorakiest, but for all that, Miss S. has made the
fatal mistake of overstating her case.
* * #
Adelaide is less “ loyal ” than Melbourne
as judged by their respective tariffs for offending
Government House. In Adelaide a man gets a
month for hooting Kintore, but in Melbourne the
Pantonites shunted an ignorant fellow for the same
term for bluffing a mere Governor’s-orderly.
* * #
Mrs. Goe, Melbourne’s Bishop’s wife, is a
hard-headed, sensible woman, reminding one a
little of Anthony Trollope’s Mrs. Proudie. Her
only weakness is jewellery, and she presides at the
Melbourne Women’s Hospital Committee meeting
with five expensive rings on her fingers, one a fine
single diamond, another a bunch of diamonds,
the third a bunch of pearls. Consequently her
colleagues put their best fingers foremost, and the
table looks like a jewellers’ shop as the diamond
rings meander over the cheque-books,
* * #
A Queensland paper announces in its
advertising columns that a “ a young gentleman,
bright, refined, without friends, wishes to be
adopted.” We trust somebody may take this
gentleman up and be a mother to him for the rest
of his life. Also if some affectionate person would
like to adopt a large, hoarse orphan of 37 and
bring him up by hand, we can put her on the
track of such a person at once. He has a reliable
thirst, and generally comes home late and raises
promiscuous Sheol among the furniture ; and any
delicate widowed lady who is yearning for some-
one to comfort her lovely soul and withdraw her
thoughts from the sad memories of the past,
would find him invaluable,
# * #
The widow of Actuary Black, late of the
A.M.P. Society, has requested that no further
steps be taken concerning the proposed gratuity,
and the ballot has consequently been abandoned.
It would have been well for Insurance had it never
been proposed. No more insidious blow could
have been aimed at the system than to make it
seem necessary that the widow of its most highly-
paid officer and stoutest polemic upholder should
require eleemosynary aid on his decease. “ Surely
an enemy hath done this thing.”
* # *
The life of the American Postmaster-
General is insured for £260,0C0. But nobody
would assure the life of N.S.W. Postmaster-
General O’Connor for over £5 since he put up
those red light-houses to advertise pills and whisky
at the street corners.

Governor Hopetoun attended a cattle-show
the other day, and was walking round in a stately,
HOW HE WENT INTO THE WHALE.
HOW HE COULD NOT PAY.
HOW HE GOT OUT AGAIN.
These are stone tablets recently found at an excavation at Ancient Askalon and supposed to represent the legend of Jonah in

ofiicial manner accompanied by his suite, when
somebody slapped him on the back with great
violence. He turned round in haste to inspect
the aggressor, and found a black gin pounding on
the vice-regal spine in the guileless ignorant
manner peculiar to her race. It was an embarras-
sing situation, and when the lady shook hands
with him it didn’t improve matters much. She
was a stout, middle-aged person, dressed in a coal-
bag and a petticoat, and her muddy feet obscured
most of the polish on his boots. Also she was of
a reserved turn of mind and offered no coherent
observation, so Hopetoun attempted to relieve
the moral tension by giving her half-a-crown.
Then a great, glad smile of recognition passed over
the countenance of the daughter of the soil, and
diving into a pocket that was concealed some-
where about her knees she solemnly handed out
an infected sixpence by way of change, and went
her way.
* # *
The Melbourne Young Women’s Christian
Association announces that its hired emissary goes
round those factories where girls are employed
and spreads the good tidings at feeding-time, and
then it gratefully mentions that arrangements
have been made in many cases to curtail the lunch-
hour in order to give time for religious instruction.
If the Y.M.O.A. could imagine the awful language
of some of these girls when they find their meal
cut short so that they may listen to some facts
about Barrabas, it would probably feel just a
shade less joyful about its great mission.

count in Queensland. An Alligator Creek “ cock-
atoo ” advertises in a Townsville paper for a
tutor, “ who must be able to milk ! ”
* * *
A good deal of iuss is still being made in
Sydney about Parson Gardiner’s affair. Why ! the
people of Sydney have raised a statue to a good
churchman, who was known in his good church
days to have seduced two sisters, and he was a
married man, too !
* * *
The N.S. Wales “ junior bar ’’ gets a
nasty knock in the new Probate Bill, where
attorneys, solicitors, and proctors get the same
right of audience as do barristers. The “2 guas.”
for bobbing up with a slip of paper, supposed to
be a last will and testament, with “Yer Honor,
probate, last will and t’st’ment ” of John Smith,
&c., will now go into the capacious maw of the
family solicitor—who won’t send to the barrister
money which he can himself pocket. The senior
bar and the solicitors have in this matter coalesced
to starve the junior bar.
# * *
Not long ago an Australian Supreme Court
judge complimented a junior barrister on the
manner in which he had conducted a divorce-case.
The following week the barrister married the
judge’s daughter!
* * *
Dear Bulletin, —Most of your readers
will probably consider Bunyip’s yarn, “ Stuffing,”
an amusing invention, but from my own experi-
ence not long ago, I have little doubt about its
truth. People travelling through countries largely
populated by Chinese are generally surprised how
cleverly the Mongols use the well-trained muscles
of the mouth for various kinds of work. The
laundrymen, for instance, are capable of stowing
away in their capacious mouths nearly half-a-pint
of a thin starchy solution, which while ironing,
with faces like Seraphs playing the trombone, they
squirt in a fine spray continually over the clothes,
one mouthful being sufficient for a large number
of shirts. A short while ago, while staying at a
station in the Great Pine-Scrub, I was strolling
about the yard when through the half-open
kitchen-door my eye was involuntarily caught by
the sight of the fat Chinese cook, with his cheeks
blown up in the characteristic way, ready for im-
mediate action, I suspected that he might be
starching the table-cloth for dinner, and stopped a
moment to ascertain how far one could go in the
use of the napkins that night. But I was mis-
taken, Ah Hop was not ironing, he was
handling a splendid cake and was artistically
putting on—the icing! I was uncertain if I
should say anything about my discovery, but,
besides being aware that the people counted
amongst the most ardent believers in Chinese
labour, I was but slightly acquainted with them,
and, for all I knew, they might have been fully
aware of this style of cooking, and might be
entirely free from prejudices. For, to do the cook
justice, when the cake made its appearance at the
dinner-table, it looked very tempting, the icing
being applied in a very even frothy layer. Judging
by the way the diners, the ladies especially, helped
themselves to it, it must also have been of excel-
lent flavour, but here I cannot speak from my own
experience. “Try the cake, I can really recom-
mend it! ” said the pretty governess, audibly
crunching between her pearly teeth a piece of the
artistic icing. But “ I hardly ever eat cake,” and
preferred a banana. Yours, New-Chum.
# # *
A certain amount of surprise has been
created in Brisbane by the erratic conduct of a
lady who has altered her complexion more sud-
denly than usual. For at least ten years the
patient has been known in society as a handsome
brunette, and now she has suddenly started busi-
ness as a blonde, with a great mass of golden hair.
A change of this kind ought to be effected by
degrees; when it is done instantaneously the
result is too surprising. Some eight or ten years
ago one of the beauties of Bendigo was a barmaid
with a tinge of milk and roses in her cheeks, and
hair the colour of yellow sunshine, but she mar-
ried one Aaron, and faded away almost in a day.
No one ever knew how the transformation came
about, but it is presumed that the bridegroom
threw the complexion out of the window, and put
his foot down on the golden wash, for within 48
hours after the marriage there was nothing left
but a yellow girl with hay-coloured tresses, and
all the glory had departed.

Sir Bernard Burke announces per circular
that he has in the press vol. I. of “ Burke’s
Colonial Gentry :
A Genealogical and Heraldic Dictionary of the Prin-
cipal Families Residing in the British Colonies, By Sir
Bernard Burke, C. 8., LL.D., Ulster King of Arms.
Author of the Dictionary of the Peerage and Baronetage,
The Landed Gentry, Vicissitudes of Families, Reminis-
cences, Ancestral and Anecdotal, &c., &c.
This strikes us as somewhat premature, inasmuch
as we are not yet in possession of a reliable Aus-
tralian Newgate Calendar. It also appears
rather ill-timed, considering how energetically the
canvassers for “Men of Mark ” are just now being
thrown downstairs by the public.
# * *
Sydney Echo has offered five guineas for
the best original sailing song. It is said that there
are but forty good stories, and that thirty-nine of
these cannot be told in the presence of ladies.
That, if one may judge from the ditties trolled at
the Sydney harbour sailing-camps, is also the case
with nautical songs. No sailing song to suit
“the boys ” will, it may safely be wagered, ever
be printed in so godly a little paper as the Echo.
# * #
The champion mean man just now is a
certain N.S.W. assayer, who went out to inspect
a mine and, getting benighted, was afforded shel-
ter in the tent of a working miner and allowed to
sleep on the latter’s only couch—two half-hags of
fresh chaff. When the digger returned from his
night-shift the assayer had decamped, leaving no
word of thanks, and before going had turned out
the chaff to his horse, who had got away with the
bulk of it.
* * *
As a proof of the extent of the buffalo-
shooting industry in the Northern Territory, it
is mentioned in the Port Darwin Times that
three trappers on the Adelaide river got two
hundred hides in just about a month. The beef
is utterly wasted. Here is a hint to the sportsmen
of the south.
* * *
The same venerable story over again. Mr
Avison, of Daylesford (Vic.), came home un-
expectedly, and got into the house so suddenly
that Mr. George Hoare hadn’t time to get out
Mr. Avison had a wild, infuriated glare in his
eye, and carried an axe. He reproved Mr. Hoare
on the head with this implement* and any brains
which that gentleman ever possessed were so much
loosened that he will never want them again. A
man who doesn’t know enough to put a chest of
drawers up against the door should stay at home.
# * #
That angel visitant who searches the
pockets of coarse, unfeeling man while he is
asleep, and gives him her bonnet-bill to pay, i s
just waking up to a sense of another grievance
and when Parkes is quite through with his Female
Suffrage Billhe might take a look at this new
problem. When a winsome woman marries she
is required to assume her husband’s name, and
thus sink her own identity, and she is beginning to
ask why this should be thus. She may be a
queenly creature with a patrician designation
and he a sawn-off, stumpy person called Briggs’
but all the same the tyranny of custom demands
that she should also become Briggs, and the angel
visitant feels that this is rough. Consequently
it is suggested that the name should be a matter
of previous arrangement, the wife to take that of
the husband or the husband that of the wife, as
may be agreed upon, or—if they should’ so
resolve—the two names may both remain, and
Miss Smith, on her marriage with Mr. Jones
may simply become Mrs. Smith, while he remains
Jones as before. As the lady, no matter what she
is called, will doubtless go through her husband’s
pockets just as before, he can afford to let her
have her way on this point; the result will be
very much the same.

# # *
A painful sight this hot weather is that of
a red-headed man in a shiny bell-topper and
a black suit trying to look cool.
* # #
The Rev. Bigamist M‘Leod, who is appeal-
ing to the Privy Council, is still incarcerated at
Darlinghurst prison, and until very recently was
clothed and fed as an ordinary convict. The in-
justice of this was, however, apparent, and he has
been allowed to resume his own clothing and buy
his own food. Which reminds us that at Sydney
August Quarter Sessions a bush man got eight
years for something about a cheque. This terrible
punishment was awarded on the strength of some
previous heavy sentences “read-up” against the
man. These, it has since been admitted, were
read up in error. What has been done with the
man?
# # #
A Queensland Anglican church-verger
“ slung out ” the organist during divine service,
the other day, under the delusion apparently that
he had been looking at the wine (the Sacramental
wine) when it was red. Sequel: verger ordered
to pay 40s. fine and costs, in default 14 days.

# # *
“ Eligibles ” are almost as scarce in
Northern social circles as in the average back-
block village, while sighing maidens, on the other
hand, are quite as proportionately numerous and
willin’. Thus, when a man who is in sassiety, as
was a certain prominent doctor, smiles on the
girls, then goes away deliberately and marries a
nurse, the indignation of society knows no bounds.
Nor is the case mended when it casually trans-
pires that the engagement was at least a couple of
years old. Tis said that the ordinary, well-
disposed, meddlesome kind of person called to
remonstrate with the doctor, and was ejected from
the verandah with a velocity that carried him
round the corner of the next street.
# *
Rupert Oarington is still in Melbourne
waiting to get married. In London, Rupert’s
name is a mere synonym for all the ennobling
accomplishments which can adorn a brainy human
being. Great things may be predicted of this
young man when he gets enough money to fully
indulge his scientific tastes in his old English
haunts.
* # *
An Anglican clergyman writes to a Sydney
paper to show that the clergy are not so badly
paid as has been asserted. With very few ex-
ceptions, says he, incumbents of churches receive
at least £4OO a year, with house rent and taxes
free, and at least £5O additional from fees. More
than half-a-dozen receive £5OO a year and fees,
with house, and some two or three get more than
this by a hundred or two. Young men just
ordained, in deacon’s orders, receive as curates
not less than £l5O. But they are unmarried men,
and have no one but themselves—certainly no
horses—to keep.
* * *
The Collingwood drapery firm of Foy
cuts off a gas-bill of £lOOO a year, by adopting
the electric-light.
* * *
General Booth reckons that his first batch
of hallelujah paupers will arrive in Australia
about twelve months from this date. The interval
will give Australia j ust about time enough to blow
the dust out of the old gun, and grind the axe,
and give a large order for coffins, and otherwise
clear the decks for action.
# * *
The hypodermic blush is much in vogue
just now, and in the case of dramas where the
moral is rather too suggestive it is absolutely in-
dispensable. It is brought about by injecting a
little colouring-matter into each cheek, and for
two hours it gives the wearer a look of offended
sensibility and soft confusion. Then it fades
away, and leaves her with a wild, greeny-yellow
aspect, as if her dinner were in insurrection on
the surface of an internal sea, and she is blinded
by the glare of her own nose, for the colour rushes
to that organ, and resists any known form of
medical treatment short of murder. Ten years
hence there will be an impression abroad that
beauties of 1890 were a hard-drinking race, and
many of them will adopt the faith of St. Peter,
and hide an hopelessly inflamed proboscis in a
nunnery.
* # *
A big section of the Sydney evening press
has evidently made up its mind that Larsen,
accused of the Peak Hill murder, shall not have a
fair trial. It is engaged in prejudicing his trial
on the capital charge by giving ex parte evidence
proving that the accused was a professional
robber. Schmidt’s case, and a thousand other
cases, over again.
* * *
News comes of the formation of a
“Kreutzer Sonata Club,” composed entirely of
men who have been unfortunate in their
domestic relations, and whose object is to dis-
courage others from “committing matrimony.”
At present this charming club consists of less
than a dozen members ; but as these have already
got into trouble for forcibly trying to prevent a
marriage by the primitive device of kidnapping
and drugging the bridegroom, it will be seen that
they make up in energy what they lack in
numbers.
# * *
The tale of how Prince George of Wales
and one companion knocked six wild ruffians into
the dust in the streets of Montreal has suddenly
gone kite-high, and an awful horror has taken its
place. The fight was genuine enough, but it was
the Prince who was knocked out. There had been
an Imperial drunk in the Canadian city, and
about 1 a.m. George and two supporters were
making the early morning hideous with three
great horse-laughs in a public thoroughfare, but
when they finished up by shoving two compositors
into the gutter, these individuals grew angry and
walloped the majesty of Britain. Then George of
Wales screamed vociferously for the police, and
the whole five were run in. The affair was
squared up in the morning, and the printers were
bought off and sworn to eternal secresy, but, un-
luckily, the constables who arrested a banged and
bruised prince at a street corner didn’t get a fair
share of the hush-money, and they seem to have
given the story away.
* * *
As a result of the passing of Shiels’ Divorce
Act in Victoria, there are some forty cases “ listed ”
for the coming sittings. Drunkenness and deser-
tion are the grounds chiefly alleged.
9 # *
The committee - room of Melbourne
Women’s Hospital is adorned by what the visitor
at first takes to be a fine oil-painting of Genevieve
Ward, the actress, but it turns out to be not
Genevieve, but only dear Lady Loch. Yet
Genevieve’s performance of “Antigone” raised
£5OOO for the magnificent new wing, and what
performance did dear Lady Loch ever give ?
However, the committee is strict, and has to keep
Thespiana in the background.
# # *
The latest English sensation is a double
murder committed by a woman named Pearsey,
who, being jealous of the wife and child of a man
named Hogg, with whom she had long been
unduly intimate, decoyed them to her house,
killed them both and carted the bodies off the
premises in the dead child’s perambulator. After
reading the details of this terrible affair, The
Bulletin is confirmed in its opinion that alf

The fruit-hawking trouble begins again in
Melbourne, the city Corporation, as mouthpiece
only of the shopkeepers, having, in the course of
the winter, fulminated regulations to gag the
truckers utterly, and drive the working-classes to
shops where an article of half the value is sold at
double the price. Here, if you view it aright, is an
aspect of the great Labour-war.
Billy Little.
The most popular man in the Queensland Assembly
is Billy Little, member for that immense tropical
constituency, Woothakata. Billy says of himse
that he is an Irishman, born in Australia. He
has been miner, bullock-driver, publican, store-
keeper, butcher, and many other things by turns ;
he now acknowledges no other occupation than
that of representative of the miners and carriers
in the Far North. He varies his industrious
attention to Parliamentary duty by a sincere study
of current sporting information, thus keeping ms
already profound knowledge on those subjects in
touch with latest developments. _ He is the sole
authority in the Queensland Legislature upon the
vast and complicated details of the Turf, prize-
ring and kindred subjects. He is all-Australian
in his sporting sympathies ; outside of Australia,
in his opinion, sport does not exist. While he
has a generous appreciation of individual effort,
he has a lofty and serene consciousness that in the
field of his favourite studies there is only one man
capable—did he choose to put forth his might—iff
deserving the laurels. That man is Billy Little.
It must be allowed that if there were a cake tor
boasting, Billy would take it. It must also be
conceded that his record to some extent justifies
his vaunts. This was tacitly admitted by some
members lately in the smoking-room. I hey told
Billy they didn’t believe he could fight, and that
they were sure his statements on the subject were
all blow. Billy’s reply was concise. He said :
“Hit me.” They didn’t.
# ' * *
Billy Little is a broad-shouldered, battered-
looking old fellow, with a great mop of bleached
hair on his head, and with black eyes that twinkle
with humour, glow with enthusiasm, or blaze
with fury as the mood is on him; he has as many
scars as a veteran and possesses a limp among the
memorials of his long life. The sources of his
popularity in the Assembly are many. He has
unflagging spirits, a rough but very rich humour,
he is transparently honest, he belabours his anta-
gonists with sincerity but without vindictiveness;
.there is no trace of malice in his composition, and
he has the genuine Australian spirit of helpfulness
towards a neighbour in trouble. In fact, in a
rough, bullocky, odd, humorous way, Billy goes
near fulfilling the spirit of a sermon that was once
preached on a mountain.
# # *
Billy Little’s contributions to debate are not
frequent, but they are always listened to with
attention - and with laughter. In high-pitched
staccato tones lie flings out in all directions. But
while provoking general merriment he usually con-
trives to add something valuable to the fund of wis-
dom and information on the subject he deals with.
It is in interjection, however, that he is unrivalled.
The essence of a whole debate is sometimes con-
tained in one of his racy remarks. During the
recent discussion on _ Griffith’s decentralization
measure, MTlwraith, in the course of his speech,
dealt with the financial view of the proposed
Northern separation scheme. He laboured to
show that the North would be crushed by her
share of the Queensland debt. The Northern
members had, as they thought, shown over and
over again how the difficulty could be _meb, and
they were impatient at this, to them, irritating
playing of the same old tune. “What,” asked
the Treasurer, “will you do with this debt.
“ I’ll give you my cheque for it,” sang out Billy
from a remote corner, and the contemptuous
humor of the remark, so well conveyed by the
high-pitched voice, delighted the Northern mem-
bers and caused MTlwraith to laughingly drop
the subject.
* * *
Billy Little’s adieu to Sir Henry Norman is
likely to become historical. Here it is, almost
literally: “Well, Sirenery, good-bye,_ bongvoige,
and when you go to tea at Windsor give my love
to Victoria. What the red fluid hot place are you
fellows laughing at ? ” The latter part of this
speech was addressed to the assemblage, consisting
of the Ministry, aides-de-camp and other creamy
people who were standing around the departing
Governor.
* * *
There is one subject that is always certain to
provoke Billy Little’s fiercest _ rage. It is the
Cairns-Herberton railway. This monstrous job is
in Billy’s electorate, and has been perpetrated in
the teeth of popular opinion just to please certain
political wire-pullers in Cairns. The railway is
conducted over what is probably the steepest and
roughest range of mountains in Australia. I
tell you, mister,” Billy roars when he is on the
subject, “it won’t do. It will never do. Why,
the gradients are so steep that you would want to
put a sanguinary ‘ britchen ’ on the ditto_ engine
coming down them.” It is hard to say if Billy
will appear in a future Parliament, for Northern
constituencies love variety. If he should, how-
ever, the new House will have assured to it two
elements rare enough in these enlightened times—
a spice of original and genuine humour, and a
fine scorn of humbug and hypocrisy.
South Australia must either have a disso-
lution or a coalition before long.
Cardinal Newman had a contempt for
women, so writes one of his biographic intimates
in a magazine to hand. The Cardinal did not go
the length of the Psalmist, “ All men are liars,”
but was inclined to think all women were. We
have heard an Australian Chief Justice say the
same thing : “ I never believe the uncorroborated
statement of a female witness.
The friends of the late Mr. John Suther-
land, M.L.A., have engineered a Treasury vote
of £3OOO for his widow through the N.S.W.
Asembly, on the usual grounds. The grant would
have come with better grace from a Parliament to
none of the members of which had Sutherland ever
lent any money, which the borrowers, instead of
repaying, had included in their insolvency sche-
dules.
According to G. R. Sims, the Gattis (rich
Swiss caterers) sent 300 Conservative Swiss from
London to Berne by special train, paying all
expenses and finding them in pocket-money, to
vote at the late plebiscitical election. It is satis-
factory to know that their money was thrown
away. All this, as it happened at a distance,
reads queerly here, but wherein does the occur-
rence differ from the sending of special trains full
of non-resident property-voters to outlying con-
stituencies at every Australian general election?
While the strike was on, the
capitalists-made a lot of noise about
the peace of the country. Now they
want the whole of it again as usual.
An Indian hawker, suspected
of murdering a comrade in the
Gippsland district, has been already
convicted, sentenced and hanged by
the Melbourne newspapers. Which
reminds us that another Indian
person, who supplied the aforesaid
hawkers with their gaudy merchan-
dise, makes no distinction between
the Koran and the Bible for “swear-
ing ” purposes. The ghosts of those
old “martyrs,” who used to get
burnt up on mere technical points
of Christian religion, must be gradu-
ally finding out what idiots they
were.
Bulwer Lytton, in “ The
Caxton,” has a pretty “bit,” to the
effect that some men plant in their
hearts a tree of love, beneath
whose shadow no flowers (the little
“annuals”) of happiness can
flourish. It seems that the re-
cently - deceased Alphonse Karr
wrote—fifty years ago—“ Love re-
sembles those trees beneath whose
shadow all vegetation dies.” Had Lytton been
studying the Frenchman, or was this similarity
merely a literary “ coincidence ?”
As a hair dressing and renovator, Ayer’s Hai r
Vigor is universally commended. It eradicates
dandruff, cures eruptions of the scalp, invigorates
and beautifies the hair, and prevents its fading or
turning gray.


An exhilarating cordial is Wolfe’s Schnapps.
A Servian hotel-keeper lately published
the following obituary of his wife in all the papers
of the country :
With a heart full of sadness I hereby give notice of the
death of my beloved wife Sophia, who died by her own
hand, aged 32 last Sunday. For nine years we lived
aappily together, and to me in her youth and beauty she
was ever as a flower laden with the dew of early morning,
an ornament to my home, and the pride of my heart.
Last winter the Evil One sent a wicked major to my
house, who persecuted my poor wife until he succeeded in
seducing her innocent heart. When 1 found them out,
my beloved Sophia was so filled with shame at her sin
that she fired a revolver at herself, thus redeeming her
good name, but leaving me an unhappy man for the rest
of my life.”
After this poetical communication, the bereaved
husband declares that should he succeed in finding
Sophia’s major he will certainly give him up to
the police.
Wolfe’s Schnapps will make you a new man.

Notice to Artistic and Literary Contributors.
Strictly Original matter contributed to The Bulletin
will be paid fur. The Editor will be glad to receive and
consider contributions as under : —
1. Original humorous or political matter, illustrated or
otherwise. (Drawings must be made in clean lines
of Indian ink on white paper or Bristol-board.)
0. Unpublished anecdotes of living celebrities, or people
who ought to be celebrated.
S. Humorous poems (from eight to forty-eight lines pre-
ferred ).
U. Serious poems, of similar or greater length, especially
those suitable for pictorial illustration.
5. Short Australian or other stories, up to, say, 3
'words. About half that length, or even less,wil
he preferred.
All Contributions must be Written Legibly, on One
Side Only of the paper, and folded, not rolled. 2 he
name and address of the writer must appear, in all
tases, on the MS. itself, and not merely on an accompany-
ing letter or paper. The Proprietors do not hold them-
selves responsible for Dejected Contributions ; but, ij
accompanied by a stamped and addressed envelope or
wrapper, every effort unit be made to return them.
The Editor does not undertake to enter into any
correspondence respecting rejected Manuscripts.

hybrid x’aces.”
John Burns says that the old Unionists
were physically larger than the new, and were
attired so “respectably ” in high hats, black coats,
and thick watch chains that they presented an
aldermanic appearance. Owing to the greater
intensity of toil during the last thirty years, the
“new” delegates look like workmen and dress
like workmen.

Mortgage, Discount, and Deposit Bank, of 4

secured the able services of Mr. Henry A. Birk-

be fairly dealt with. In these days of extortion, the
above institution should about fill a long-felt
want.*

The largest gold coin in circulation is the
“ loof ” of Annam, value £65. “ Loof,” in Scotch,
means the palm of the hand. , The Annamese
“loof ” is the coin to palm.

Camille Desmoulins : “As a youth I lost
my belief in a future life, but the troubles of the
world forced it upon me until I got married,
when my perfect happiness led me to disbelieve
that there could be anything better.” Ultimately
he was guillotined, and his wife followed, rtobe-
spierre and Brissot were witnesses at the
riage as Desmoulins’ friends, but he helped to
send Brissot to the scaffold, and Robespierre sent
him there.
WHAT EXPERIENCE TEACHES
IS BEST.
There is something to consider in the daily
expenses for the household necessities, which in
the aggregate of the year amount to a good deal.
The ethics of buying and selling appear to be for
the vendor to get all he can, and for the purchaser
to see that he don’t get too much. But what i s
too much ? It is almost a profound question, and
presents the strange paradox that the dearest in
many things is often the cheapest. Certainly this
is so in food, when health is involved, and in
remedies which restore health after it has been
impaired. It is certainly so in clothing; for a
cheap suit that will hardly last one season is dearer
than the one which will last two seasons, the
difference in price being reasonable. So in shoes
and the like. Recurring to the items of food and
health, undoubtedly the most important, it is
found that villainous adulteration is what renders
cheap much that is sold, and men are
known who have spent thousands to be cured of
disease, have suffered years of agony, and have
trifled away their substance on worthless remedies.
That which is testified to by thousands as being
an absolute cure, and permanently efficacious, is
cheap at any price in comparison with such as
have no virtue, and which prolong suffering. A
case in point is the following : “New Bloomfield,
Pa., April 26, 1886. The Charles A. Yogeler Co.,
Baltimore, Md. Gentlemen, —For more than
thirty years I had been afflicted with rheumatism
so severe I had to use morphine to secure rest at
night. Spent hundreds of dollars with physicians
and for remedies without benefit. Five years ago
I tried St. Jacobs oil, and it effected an entire and
permanent cure. I have not been troubled with t
since. Cold or damp weather does not affect me
at all. I desire to give it my unqualified endorse
ment.—J. E. Bonsall, clerk of the several Courts
of Perry County, Pa.” The point here is not so
much what Mr. Bonsall paid for the great remedy
for pain, for the price is a mere bagatelle,' but that
he was permanently cured after 30 years’ suffering.
Of course the poor must count the cost in every-
thing ; but they should reckon on the sound basis
that that which is bad is worthless, and that
which is the best and will cure, and stay cured, is
cheap at any price.
A TEXAS SIFTING.
F. G. Presby, Marshall, Texas, April 14, ’9O,
writes the Waterbury Watch Co. as follows: —
“ Though a stranger to you, I wish to let you
know something-that happened to me in the
spring of 1889 on account of one of your Water-
bury Watches. I left my home'in Pensacola City,
Fla., to find a new one farther west. Coming to
llnver, Colo., and having travelled all over the
Rocky Mountains without any watch in my
pocket, as I was coming one day from my place to
Denver, Colo., after I had got my place in order,
I came to look over the city. My intention was
to go to Kansas City, Mo. I passed one watch-
maker’s store on Lariraer-street, in Denver, and
saw there a watch in the window for only 2dol.
50c. I went in and I bought one, paid, the money
for it, and left the city next day for Kansas City,
Mo., in the 1 p.m. train. After I was about one
hour's ride from the city the conductor of the
train came and took a seat by my side.
I asked the conductor the _ time, and as a
gentleman he told rno the time, and I said I
also have my watch to tell me the time. As soon
as the gentleman (conductor) of the train espied
my watch he commenced to laugh, and told me at
the same time that he could get a bushel of such
trash aS Waterbury Watches, all he wanted, for
one dollar, while I had paid 20dol 50e. But I did
not lose my courage, and told the conductor that
I would bet a cigar, only first that I was not a
betting man, and secondly that my Waterbury
Watch for 2.50d0l was just as good as an Elgin or
any other 250.00d0l watch, in regard to keeping
good time. The conductor accepted the bet.
Nearing Kansas City next day I notified the con-
ductor about our bet. When we reached Kansas
City my 2.50d0l Waterbury Watch was correct
and the conductor’s watch from Elgin, 111., was H
minutes behind time, so the Waterbury Watch
won the bet. I have received the cigar, have set
fire to it, and am enjoying the smoke.”

To H. F. Kugelma.nn, Esq.
Dear Sir,—This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, which
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. I consulted
wore than one doctor , and during that time took a great
ijl lantity Of medicine without it doing me the slightest
good, but I became worse every day, wasting away until
I became a perfect skeleton. About February last I began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and severe
pains in my left lung, and the quantity of bad matter I
was expectorating every day, I became very much alarmed.
Hearing of your professional skill I consulted you, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. I agreed
and went under your treatment in March last. At the
end of three months I was taken bad one morning, and
vomited about two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. From that time I commenced to improve, and
was gaining in weight every day. About a month ago the
blood ceased coming, the cough disappeared, and the
lung healed completely, and thank God and your pro-
fessional skill, I am as well as ever.
P. KEENAN.
Declared and subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty’s
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick, in the
County of Grant, this 10th day of December, 1888.
Peter Smytiik, .T.P.

The Referee.
The appearance jot Burge and Dawson in a
Sydney Police Court charged with prize-fighting, and the
numerous letters, silly in every case, on both sides of the
question that have appeared in the daily press, have
directed a deal of public attention towards the “nobie
art.” If the goody-goodies are to be believed, boxing is
ruinous to both body and soul ; according to the other
crowd the only sure passport to eternal glory. Indeed,
the secretary of a Sydney club-has been rude enough to
contradict Queen Victoria, declaring that the pluck en-
gendered in the prize-ring is the _ secret of England s
greatness, and has also bad the brassiness to aver that be
doesn’t believe that anyone was ever killed 01 perman-
ently injured in a well-Conducted I> oxi ll g -match.
Broken ribs, smasbed-up knnekhs, flattened noses,
scarred faces, toothless gums, and cars that look ~s if a
dog had chewed ih >m, are incontrovertible coni radii 'ions
of the lat ter part ef this extraordinary statement, and no
one need go beyond his own personal experience nr
reading for testimony to upset the former. But some-
thing could lie done to lessen the brutality of the sport
The success of John L. Sullivan has set every boxei
aflame with but o,ne idea—“ knock out.” Let future con-
tests be for points. Point-fighting may seem tame at
first after the bashing business, but public taste can be
educated up to anything. One of the worst features of
the present.system is the three-minute rounds with ten
seconds allowed to knocked-ddwn men. Perhaps it is not
necessary to shorten the rounds, but a “ Knock-down ”
should in all cases terminate the round, and the fallen
man should be allowed the minute interval in his corner.
Modern fighting, Burge, Slavin, and Goddard to wit, is
chiefly a matter of strength and condition—skill has little
to do with it.
* # #
Remarkable, isn’t it, that when Burge and
Dawson appeared at Sydney Central charged with prize-
fighting none of the influential promoters of the fight
were summoned as 00-defendants? The very essence of
a prize-fight (and therein lies its noxiousness in the eyes
of the law) is the presence of interested spectators. As
fights for money never take place without spectators, and
as the prize-fighting-law is not intended to reach cases
where men settle sudden disputes by force of fists, it is
plain that the backers are really as legally culpable as are
the so-called principals.
# * #
The poverty of England explains away her
backward position in pugilism. With the middle and
lighter weights she can still hold, her own, as witness
Carney in America. But she has no heavy-weight, for
Burke and Charley Mitchell properly trained would be
well within the middle division. Even that exploded
fraud, Jem Smith, is not a big man. This dearth is
accounted for by the fact that- the agricultural population
has migrated to the towns, and physique has so declined
that even the army authorities have been forced to reduce
the standard for height and chest measurement. Among
the lower ranks in England a tall, firmly-built man is now
the exception. Then, the Irish, who have always con-
tributed more than their share to the P.R., have, for
reasons “more fiscal than Popery,” been driven to
America and Australia.
# # *
The well-known and cheery William Midwinter,
a fine cricketer and billiard-player, a great athlete in his
day and a lion hitter, has just died in Kew Asylum
(Melb.) of paralysis—directly induced by grief consequent
on the loss of his wife and children. But let the truth be
told for the benefit of young men with big chests. As an
animal, Midwinter was a magnificent man—a splendid
specimen of the English yeoman, whom the greed of the
English landlord - is scattering all over the world. Mid-
winter was a professional athletic success, and so gravi-
tated to the landlordship of a public-house. No man can
face grief as the landlord of a public-house if he drinks
with his customers. Grog is the enemy of the athlete-
above all, it is the enemy of the athlete who presumes on
his strength.
# * *
No fault can*be found with the selection of the
N.S-W. International Cricket Team. Even in the absence
of Turner and Garrett it is remarkably strong, and it is
quite refreshing to see the mother colony represented by
eleven performers and no “paper” men. However, South
Australia will probably come out on top. The Southerners
have for long been asserting their progress in cricket and
with such an artist as George Giffen for mentor, it is little
wonder. Then there is Lyons, acknowledged in England
as the fastest scorer of the age, and, upon running down
the names of the rest of the men who compose the S.A.
team, it looks as if the time has now come when they are
successfully to claim the championship.
# * •
Here is some phenomenal scoring. Against S.
Adelaide the other day Norwood made 889 for 3 wickets.
George Giffen made 253 not out. At the other end of the
same Oval, Adelaide made 333 for one wicket Hiscock 143,
Evans 123, both not out). This means that on the
Adelaide Oval on one day 722 runs were made for 4
wickets, two of them run out! Does not this prove that
senior cricket on latter-day turf wickets is a big farce,
from a bowler’s point of view? Why not provide the
batsmen with broom-handles, and let the bowlers shoot
at them with a cannon ?
# # *
Bteeplechasing does not “catch on” with the
N.S.W. amateurs, as evinced by the fact that, at Rosehill
ori Saturday, out of 28 horses entered for the Amateur
Steeplechase, only three faced the starter. One baulked
after going a couple of furlongs, and his rider not per-
severing, the race resolved itself into a match between
Cymbal and Longfellow. The latter fell, hut Mr. Prender-
gast remounted and finished the course without stirrups,
very close to Cymbal. His pluck seems enhanced when
it is remembered that on Tom Hales losing a stirrup on
Admiral at the V.R.C. meeting, he tried to pull the colt
up, and fell off in the attempt.
* # *
To any regular attendant at Sydney suburban
races, it is patent that the A.J.O. should appoint a
detector of “ stiff ’uns,” even at the risk of its being said
that the action was a plagiarism of the action of that
excellent institution, the V.R.C. Never, perhaps, was
the need of such an official more strongly exemplified
than on Saturday, when Mr. “ Johnny Armstrong ”
was fairly on the job. That the surroundings of the
“ turf ” will ever allow it to become thoroughly clean is
beyond all human,expectation, but,- with.strict super-
vision, a good deal might be done to crush the most bare-
faced of the swindles which are weekly perpetrated on
the proprietary courses about Sydney.

Mr. “Pink ’Un” Corlett has been telling the
London Times that betting has not of late years increased,
and in support gives some interesting facts. Thormanhy S
Derby of 1860 brought the late Mr. Merry £IOO,OOB in
bets. The winnings on Macoaroni were equally large in
1163, and much larger when Hermit landed the great coup
for Machell and Chaplin, in 1867. Mr. Corlett, who
speaks with some authority, thinks that no one man lias
(luring the last 10 years won £20,000 on a Derby. Though
the Cesarewitch and the Cambridgeshire are now the great
betting races, he thinks the wagering on this year’s big
handicap small compared with what it was when the
Marquis of Hastings and his friends won‘ £200,000 over
Lecturer, in 1860. Mr. Corlett, however, loses sight of one
important fact. Betting in those days was chiefly an
iristoeratic amusement. Now, every one has his “bit
, •>,” and consequently the aggregate sum wagered must
be greater, though no bettor may win such large sums as
of yore, no penciller make such a big hook. There are
ten hookies for one of 30 years ago, and a hundred times
as many hackers.

* * *
Now that “novice” handicaps have spoilt the
N.S.W. pedestrian boom some of the crack pedestrians
should journey to England, as they are only losing time
in Australia. Tf as good as reputed, some of them should
win, for when Samuels was entered at his best he received
a liberal start. If England is inferior to us in sculling and
pugilism, why should she produce better sprinters? To
this there is a good answer. Pulling is little practised in
England and unknown outside the Tyne and Thames.
Australian superiority in pugilism has only been proved
as regards the heavy-weights. In England, however,
nearly every youth runs, either as an amateur or a pro-
fessional, and, as a rule, you can, other things being
equal, produce better men out of 40 than out of three
millions.
A down-South person named Lazarus has
just distrained on an individual called Dives for

waits.
A man committed suicide recently in
England because he had lost his wife, the lady
having eloned with a policeman. Two weeks
later the policeman suicided because he had found
her, so even in death the corpse got square with
its enemy. The lady has not suicided —in fact,
she is quite happy and contented and getting
rapidly out of her double-banked widowhood in
order to elope some more.
