And for a moment one might mark
What had been hidden by the dark,
That the head of the maiden lay at rest,
Tenderly, on the young man’s breast!
She starts—she moves—she seems to feel
The thrill of life along her keel,
And spurning with her foot the ground,
With one exulting joyous bound.
She leaps into the ocean’s arms !
Take her, Oh bridegroom old and gray,
Take her to thy protecting arms
With all her youth and all her charms !
How beautiful she is ! How fair
She lies within those arms that press
Her form with many a soft caress
Of tenderness and watchful cafe !

At corner of George and Liverpool-streets,
Sydney, on the site of the old Royal Standard,
stands the Central Courthouse Hotel, a new three*
storey building erected by W. Phillips, of Bal-
main, the architects being Shervey and Lenthall.
This hotel is built wholly and solely for the culti-
vation of a sound bar trade, and as such ranks
second to none in Sydney. It is lavishly fitted
throughout, but special mention must be made
of the costly fittings in both the private
and public bars, the ceilings and panellings of
Which are of pine relieved with cedar, which,
with the massive glass mirrors around the sides,
make the bar one of the prettiest and most
attractive in Sydney. The kitchen is at the
top of the building, which fact does away with the
unpleasant aroma so frequently found, even in
our most modern hotels. The furnishing was
entrusted to the well-known firm of A. Hall and
Co., of London, and 565 and 567 George-street,
Sydney; bar and gasfittings, by Watson and
Crane, Pitt-street; and tilings by Dobson and Co.
The popular proprietor, Mr. J. J. Leary, is deter-
mined to keep up his old reputation, and we
therefore hope that his old patrons will roll up in
numbers. His name is a sufficient guarantee for
the success of the Venture and we wish him all
that he wishes himself.*

Tinamona’s Ghost.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Wit Were passengers in a trading.vessel that lav in
vhe saucer-shaped natural basin which does duty
<as a harbour at Earotonga, and while the last
loads of oranges were being shipped we accepted
the hospitality of an old gentleman, generally
known as the Skipper, who, after many years
a bout among the Islands and in
Maori land, had finally settled down at Avarua
the principal Earotongan settlement. As we sat
in his verandah discussing whisky and other
Island matters there passed along the road in
ifont of the house a long, thin, rather bilious-
looking individual in unmistakeable clerical rig.
v I, eiy n °d ,^ ven by our host and returned
by the gentleman in black showed that there was
not much cordiality of feeling between the pair,
and When the latter had passed, our host, telling
tes that the bilious one was the resident missionary
and that he had an uncommonly good billet, pro-
ceeded to retail for our edification a few instances
of pious zeal that lacked discretion, and which
had distinguished the parsoh on his arrival to
assume the charge of the mission station.
However, he is not quite so mischievous now
as when he first came,” proceeded the Skipper
1 suppose the climate is tempering his Zealand
toning him down a bit, but even at his worst he
Was never a patch on an old devil-dodger who was
here before him, the Eeverend Wily Grab He
was a rum-’un, and no mistake. With all his oily
piety he had a keen scent for shekels, and the
natives here used to call him Parson Dollars,
Lord ! how he used to squeeze fines out of them !
~ b suppose you know that all offences against
the Decalogue, and a lot of others not included in
it, are punishable under missionary-made law here
by a graduated system of fines. For instance, the
peccadilloes of the local girls are met with a fine of
cour dollars for the first time of being found out
and one dollar for each successive offence, the
man gcnng scot free; while, if the offender is a
m ™ e( J woman, she and her paramour are in for
a 30-dollar fine each. Payment of the fine acts as
a complete whitewashing, and fully purges of all
iniquity, the erstwhile sinners being thought no
Worse of afterwards; they have paid up, and that
is an end of the whole matter.
„‘i ln^ ee ' d ) was said among his flock that old
Wily Grab seemed to be very tender to lambs and
sheep that thus returned to the fold after paying
their footing—-more so than to the apparently un-
blemished saints of his congregation who needed
hot repentance. The former brought lots of grist
to his mill, while the latter were at best but un-
profitable servants.
“I will tell you a yarn about old Wily, which
relates to one of the most screaming farces I
ever witnessed even in connection with island
missionary labours. It was one Christmas eve; I
had been away at Ngatangiia all day. on business,
and got home rather tired, but the day had been
Very hot, and I did not feel much inclined for
bed, so, after a late supper, I sat out here smoking
my pipe and sipping my toddy in the cool night
breeze. It was about one a.m., a light wind was
blowing m-shore, and the low run of the surf
and gentle swishing of the coco-palm leaves were
*u mg , Ine into a dreamy state as I smoked.
Above these I heard an occasional distant mur-
muring sound—something between a low chant
and a monotonous gabble, I could not quite
make it out, but put it down to some sing-song
service being held at the other end of Avarua;
for these natives will sometimes meet and sit
psalm-singing for half the night.
“Suddenly I beard someone open the gate of
the front paddock, and come running up to the
house panting. ‘Papelangi!’ shouted a voice;
‘ come quick; old Mohi has seen a spirit, and he
is dying!’
“Now, Mohi was the owner of this place, and
my landlord ; not a bad old sort, but very fond of
his grog when he could indulge without fear of
Wily Grab and his fines. So up I jumped and
followed the messenger.
“As I drew near to Mohi’s house the noise I
had heard was explained. Squatted on the
ground outside of the house was a crowd of natives
of both sexes and all ages, some praying, some
singing in a low tone, swaying their bodies to and
fro as they crooned—for all the world like an Irish
wake.
“ I passed through the crowd into the house,
which was also full of squatting natives, head-men
of the village, all silent and with their eyes fixed
on a couch in the corner whereon lay Mohi, quite
still, with his agonised eyes intently gazing at one
point in the roof, while an incessant gabble poured
from his lips, though his features were otherwise
fixed and motionless. At his side, and holding
one of his hands, knelt the Rev. Wily Grab, pray-
ing fervently and aloud in native lingo.
“ As I entered, the parson rose from his knees
and, extending an arm, said in an impressively-
solemn tone:
“ ’ Friend J , you have come to bid what, I
Tw^ih 18 a farewell to your old acquaintance,
Mohi. This is a sad, a solemn, an awe-inspiring
sight; one that may well bring reflection to even
the most worldly mind. Mohi has been visited by
a spirit. He is, indeed, even now possessed by
the spirit which the Lord has vouchsafed to send
to him. You remember that reprobate Tinamona,
who died here in a state of intoxication about six
months ago ? It is his spirit that has been sent to
manifest itself to poor Mohi. And, oh ! What
a precious lesson may this visitation not afford to
sinners of my flock ! Listen.
had begun his jabbering worse than ever,
and Wily connected his scattered sentences, put
them into some sort of shape and sense, and gave
them out with a running commentary in Raro-
tongan for the benefit of the assembly.
“Listen, oh my friends ! His body lies before
you there, but his spirit is wandering forth with
I inamona’s ghost into the abode of spirits. Not,
brethren' into the regions of the blest —there
Tmamona’s impure soul could never be allowed to
penetrate—but into the place of torment! the
dwelling of the damned ! ! the realm of Satan !!!
Hear him repeat the names of those he recog-
nises !”
Here Wily Grab gave out a long roll of names
of deceased members of his flock whom he deemed
to have died impenitent.
“ You hear, my friends? All vile sinners on
earth ! all lost souls in the great hereafter ! Not
one spirit has he met of those that have gone from
here m peace to rest in glory ! Let this be a lesson
to you all here present, as it must ever be to our
poor friend lying here before us, should the Lord
vouchsafe to restore him to us once more.”
“ I hen, turning to me and speaking in English :
. Friend J said he, “well has it been
written that the Lord ‘ has hid these things from
the wise and prudent and revealed them unto
babes.’ Now you, I daresay, in common with
most other Europeans, look upon these natives as
ignorant, debased, and mere children in intellect.
Yet, wonderful are the Ways of the Almighty !
and this is not the only occasion in which I have
known Him to reveal Himself in a like mysterious
manner to these His creatures, whom we look
down upon, while not condescending to make
Himself manifest in this directly personal form to
the cultured inhabitants of civilised communities.’
“ ‘ Now, I put it to you ; tell me, friend J——,
did you, in your experience, ever know any of
your fellow-countrymen to be visited in this
special, this marvellous fashion ?’
“ ‘ Why, bless my soul, yes, Mr. Grab,’ replied
I; ‘ often and often,’
“ ‘ Often ! Oh, surely not often, Mr. J . I
doubt if you can cite even one instance in which
you have been witness of such a soleihn Visitation
by the Almighty.’
“Yes, I repeat* often. I tell you, Mr. Grab,
that I have Witnessed just such another attack at
least a hundred times, and probably oftener.”
“But where, my good sir, and under whatname
was the manifestation known among men, for I
have never seen such a case recorded ?”
“ Why, in the Waikato, among the military
settlers, during the Maori war. We used to call
it H.T.’s, the horrors, delirium tremens, blue
devils, jim-jams, and it has a lot of other names
too. It used to be a very common complaint
there.”
The Reverend Wily Grab’s jaw dropped, and
he seemed dazed with horror. Bending over the
unconscious Mohi, his nose probably caught an
emanation of the spirit which possessed the poor
old fellow, for, thrusting his hand from him with
an expression of disgust, he straightened himself
stiffly and stalked majestically out of the house,
exclaiming as he Went:
( “ And have I been called from my bed at ten
o’clock of the night, and wasted my time here
till two of the morning, and all on account of a
depraved, a wretched, a bestial drunkard?”
“Thenati vesoutside seeing his sudden departure,
and noting his stern, unbending aspect, took it as
a sign that all was over and greeted him with a
loud cry. Meanwhile some signs of returning
consciousness glimmered in Mohi’s eyes.
“As the reverend gentleman vanished, a new
comer entered on the scene, namely, Ihaia, a
native missionary, one of the pillars of Wily’s
church, the most rigid disciplinarian, the most
earnest exhorter, and most crafty old sinner in the
whole of the Cook and Hervey groups, not even
excepting Wily Grab himself.
“Ihaia, in his turn, took up a position on his
knees alongside my drunken old landlord, and as
the latter seemed coming to, and I was beginning
to feel sleepy I nodded good morning to Ihaia, and
turned homeward.
“Just as I had got into the outer enclosure of
this place, a paddock in which I had a few sheep
grazing, one of them gave a sudden spring into
the air, uttered a short, sharp bleat or cry, and
fell on its side in convulsions.
“ Thinks Ito myself; these natives are a super-
stitious set of beggars, so here goes for a lark that
may even do a little good.
“So, walking back to Mohi’s house, I opened
the door, and calling to Ihaia, I said :
“ ‘ Ihaia, your prayers have been answered.
The spirit has just left Mohi, and entered into
one of my sheep. He will soon be all right now.’
“ Well, off rushed a lot of the fellows to see if
what I said was true. There, sure enough, was
the sheep, still kicking out convulsively, atid back
they tan With this confirmation true as Holy
Writ, full of wonder and admiration at the
marvellous praying powers of Ihaia.
‘‘ln the midst of their shouts of ‘Allelulia!
‘Glory to God !’ &0., suddenly Mohi sprang up
on his couch, looked round him in a dazed fashion
for a minute or two, and then, with a loud yell,
dashed out of the house and disappeared in the
bush.
“ He was away in hiding, keeping well out of
sight of everyone for three days, and then he
came back very quietly, looking most foolish and
crestfallen.
“ It seems that oh the day of his attack he had
managed to get a bottle of square gin from the
Chinaman’s store, hfid Sat doWn ih the bush ahd
drained it to the last drop, and then, when
to get home and sleep it off, had slipped in
crossing the creek and fallen into the stream, his
head, fortunately, remaining out of the water
supported by some stones. The e®ld bath had
seemingly driven all the spirit into his head, but
anyhow, after a time he managed to scramble out
and find his way home, with the after-results as I
told you.
“The affair has been a joke against poor Mohi
ever since, and the Urchins of the Island, when-
ever they pick up a bottle with any smell of liquor
hanging about it, will take it up to the old boy
and poke it under his nose, saying: ‘Here, Mohi,
have a smell of Tinamona’s ghost!’ ”
“ Old Wily Grab, too, hung his diminished head
for a long time after and was very careful to keep
as much as possible out of my way for fear of my
reminding him of the peculiar instances of divine
manifestations to benighted Islanders which he
professed to have Witnessed.
“Ihaia, on the other hand, flourished exceed-
ingly on it. What the Maoris would call his
‘Mana’ as an advocate with the powers above,
marvellously increased among the Rarotongan
Christians, and he was looked upon as possessing
a forty-parson power of prayer at least over Wily
Grab.

An Australian Edison.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.)
One morning not long ago, as I lounged in the
shade of an oak in Parramatta Park, somewhat to
my disturbance a stranger seated himself at my
side. He was apparently about 50 years of age,
with a facial expression indicative of high mental
power and great benevolence. I was in no humour
to talk, but the intruder was of a different mind,
for presently he remarked that, so far as he knew,
the oaks forming the grove wherein we sat were
amongst the oldest of their kind in Australia. To
this I gruntingly assented.
My moroseness did not, however, affect my com-
panion, for he continued: “Some say Governor
Bligh planted them, but I never met anyone who
could speak with certainty about the matter. I
am told they thought oaks would grow well here
and at Pitt Town, and in time they reckoned
there would be some timber useful for ship build-
ing. ‘Heart of oak’ sounds well, but heart of
steel is better. I see they have found a plan of
making nickel-steel for armour-plates. The inven-
tion isn’t so bad, for if a shot strikes a vessel and
penetrates the armour, the fracture is only the
size of the shot, not as would happen with ordi-
nary steel—a large and gaping hole. I have given
the matter of armour-plating a great deal of study.
It is some years since I discovered a treatment of
steel which may yet revolutionise ship-building.
The steel is combined at a fixed temperature with
a small per-centage of bismuth and lead, and to the
metal produced I have given the name of plumb-
steel. It has the quality of resisting concussion
equally well with ordinary steel, but its fracture is
horizontal and on the passage of the shot the sides
of the fissure come together again. You can at
once see the value of the invention and its
superiority for war-vessels, even over nickel-
steel. During a naval battle there would be no
danger of a vessel, that was struck, sinking; .nor
would the holes in the hull require to be plugged
up—they would close sufficiently to keep out all
but a very small quantity of water. I have sub-
mitted the scheme to the Duke of Edinburgh,
with whom I have a slight personal acquaint-
ance.”
Here my neighbour took breath, but only for an
instant. Continuing his narration he said: “I
am anxious to get the invention off my hands
speedily, as I have another which will much dis-
count its value. I suppose you know that the
Straits of Dover are only 20£ miles wide. Well,
I have invented a piece of ordnance which will
carry 23 miles and have offered it to the French
Republic. By next mail I hope to hear from
Sadi-Carnot. My idea is that the French should
erect batteries at the narrowest part of the
channel and strongly defend them from land
attack. In these batteries would be mounted a
sufficient number of my ordnance so ranged as to
dominate every inch of water as far as the English
coast, and so render the passage of ships in war
time most dangerous.”
“ The ships could get through at night-time, you
say. Not a bit of it. I propose that the whole
strait shall be swept by electric lights, so that the
darkest night will give no better aid for a vessel
to sneak through, than the brightest day. You
see what advantage we gain by closing the strait.
A. ship going to London from the South will have
to make a circuit,, right round Ireland and Scot-
land, a distance of 1300 miles more than at pre-
sent —the detour for vessels bound for the Baltic
will also bo very considerable. As soon as the
batteries are up there will be a terrible panic in
England. The days of London’s greatness a,re
over, as it will be impossible to have the chief
city of the kingdom exposed to a blockade such
as the French could establish.”
“ What’s that you say? No gun could stand
the recoil. Just so. My invention is not a gun.
The power is got from a substance I have dis-
covered, which has the property of exerting its ex-
plosive force in a line parallel with the fibres of
which it is composed, and according as these fibres
are placed hortizonally or vertically, so will the
direction of the force be horizontal or vertical.
Chemists well know of other substances of an
analogous kind. It has also the property, which
I think is exclusively its own, of exploding with
greatest effect in the direction of most resistance.
You can imagine, therefore, the instrument out of
which it is fired will not suffer any pressure from
the discharge except that of the friction, caused
by the projectile in its passage. The gun, if I
may use the term, is merely a strong cylinder open
at each end. The shot is placed in position and.
the explosive is put behind it. The effect of the
explosion is not great on the side opposite
the shot, what there is may be said to be
dissipated through the open vent at the back of
the gun. The full force is expended against
the projectile, which, as I have said, can be thrown
with precision a distance of 23 miles. A man
having some knowledge of projectiles might
possibly o'bject that the elevation of a gun to carry
the distance would need to be 45 degrees or there-
abouts. I have not worked out the question in
regard to gunpowder, but my explosive is of an
entirely different character. If you give me a
pencil I shall make the calculation for you.
“ Let x be the—Oh, you do not care about the
details. Very well, the elevation of my gun is not
more than | of a degree per mile of range, so that
to oast a shot at the foot of the Dover cliffs the
elevation would be only 17f degrees. I have not
made any recommendation to the French as to the
number of pieces they should erect, but I don’t
mind telling you that I think seventeen would be
ample to command the strait and defend it against
the fleets of the world. Yes, theideaisabig one;
I suppose it means a matter of millions to me.”
Here I was fain to remark that it was a pity
the mind that could invent so formidable an
engine of destruction, could not devise something
for the common benefit of the human race—
“ You assume,” said my companion, “that my
explosive will not benefit mankind. Well, let
that pass. As for more humble inventions, I
have many. I have studied much the solidifica-
tion of liquids, and my success has been singular.
I can turn oil into a solid state by the addition
of a small proportionate quantity of peroxide of
barkis, and can reduce the same to its fluid state
by pouring over it a small quantity of marmorate
of taxis. The enormous commercial advantages of
the discovery are obvious. The general public,
however, will best appreciate my invention in
regard to alcohol. There is less difficulty in
solidifying alcohol than any other fluid. To
one hundred parts of pure spirit I add one tenth
part of peroxide of barkis, the composition being
allowed to rest. In an hour’s time it becomes a
solid mass, having no trace of moisture, unless,
indeed, the spirit has been diluted, in which case
the water is allowed to flow off. For an hour or
so the composition is as pliable as wax, but after-
wards becomes quite hard. To get it back into a
fluid state, one-tenth part of sulphate of barkis —
not peroxide, mind you—is added with water,
according to the proof strength required. The
solid alcohol dissolves almost immediately, both
the sulphate and the peroxide evaporating during
the liquefaction of the alcohol.
“ I have treated alcohol in this way and formed
the solid substance into pills. Sulphate of barkis
is, as you know, also a solid, capable of being
worked into small lumps or pills. To make a
drink, ten pills of alcohol and one of sulphate
are dropped into a glass and water quantum
suff. added, the pills dissolve and the drink is
ready. I have also a process of mechanically com-
bining the sulphate of barkis with the solid alcohol,
so that only one kind of pill is required to make a
drink. One great advantage of the plan is that a
man may take his stimulant bit by bit that is,
he can place the pills one at a time in his mouth
and suck them. To an experienced hand like you
no further explanation is needed. This pill busi-
ness will be a great boom some day— there are
millions in it, too. I have a few more useful
inventions I can tell you of. Ah, here are some
people coming for me —servants of Dr. Godson. I
live over there, shall I introduce you?”
At this moment two persons in uniform came
towards us. “You old 1” said one, “ you’ve
given us a nice tramp.”
It seems they wanted him at the mad-house
just across the river.
H.S.
Brisbane tram-conductors’ wages have been
raised all round from 34s. to 40s. weekly—con-
ditionally that they buy themselves new clothes !
The Hon. M. H. Moreton, of Queensland,
brother of the present Earl Duoie, is one of the
remnants of the old regime who preferred honour
to pelf. He was originally in the 76th High-
landers. On one occasion, whilst quartered in a
Highland town with his regiment, Mat., then a
stripling, strolled with some brother-officers into
a public billiard-room in the town. The room in
question was situated on the third-storey. When
Mat. walked in there was a motley crowd assem-
bled, and amongst them a big, powerful bully who
had a mortal down upon everyone wearing uni-
form. The bully walked up to young Mat. and
grossly insulted him without rhyme or reason.
The Major of the regiment, who was present, at
once insisted on taking up his subaltern’s quarrel,
saying quietly to the aggressor; “I am a fair
match for you. You had better leave the
room. ” They closed, and after a big strug-
gle the Major carted the bully through the
door and chucked him bodily down to the
next landing where he alighted with a heavy smash,
breaking a few ribs and otherwise bodily damaging
himself. An action at law was the result. Heavy
daihages were given, and the only way in which
the Major could pay up was by selling his
commission and thus ruining his prospects in the
service. Mat. (just as poor a man as the Major)
at once stepped to the front, said the quarrel was
his, and his alone, and he would settle it. He
immediately sent in his papers, and the proceeds
of his commission paid the Major’s fine. The last
the present writer saw of him was as a telegraph-
post-and-wire contractor for the Government in
Northern Queensland, working hard for his tucker,
and making nothing out of the contract.
Perseverando.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
In the ripe fields of fervid noon-tide day
The reaper, Azrael, his sickle plied,
And with a flashing and impartial sweep
The mower laid two mortals side by side.
The one had reached with sure and steadfast steps
Th’ exalted summit of a guiltless life,
Whose soul unpassioned and birth-balanced
wrought
Its own environment from sin and strife.
Each kindling spark of lust his cool blood
quenched,
He fell not—a white polar star illumed
The pathway of his pure, calm Lapland-life,
And ashes fell not where no fire consumed.
And now his spirit heavenward bent its flight
And reached the portals of eternal rest,
And knocked for entrance through the heavenly
gate,
To the dominions of the faithful blest.
“Welcome !” the angel said, “you justly come
Unto this paradise of faithful men,
Who were a soldier though you knew not war,
Nor traitor proved—l greet you ! —enter then.
The other, whose inherent, swift hot blood
Had been to his aspiring soul its scourge,
Whose weaker nature ever earthward fell,
Yet battled ever to the grave’s dark verge—
Battled to vanquish an unnumbered foe
That still, unoonquered, menaced still the throne
That oft repulsed it from the citadel
Where each new siege more desperate had
grown.
Oft wounded—falling—still he trembling rose
Whose struggling spirit would not wholly yield
Submissively to sway of evil things ;
And thus he died still fighting in the field.
And he, too, knocked at the Celestial Gate
With doubtful hand yet hope unfailing still,
Conscious his life-long fight had oft been vain,
His victories few ’gainst sin and feeble will.
And when the angel to his summons came,
Full wide he flung the gate, and a rich tone
Of silver trumpets filled the heavenly air,
And hosts Celestial led him near the Throne.
“ Behold ! ” the angel cried, “ a warrior comes
Not for great deeds of vict’ry here renowned;
He oft fell vanquished, yet hath honour won,
He wins the Crown with which the brave are
crowned.”
And thus I learn that ’tis not only those
May gain th’ immortal and desired goal
Whose pure calm natures never warfare knew,
For honour crowns the vanquished, dauntless
soul.
Gippsland.

Tales of “The System.”
No. XXIII.—A Port Arthur Episode,
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
A distinguished party of visitors had
arrived at Port Arthur, agog to inspect the
Settlement. And, not to be outdone in politeness )
the whole settlement was eager to inspect the
visitors.
For affairs, you must know, had been somewhat
monotonous and unexciting of late. Whether
it was the sultry weather—it was February—or
whether it was the absence of the Commandant
“up town,” or whether it was that the Devil had
quite enough to occupy his attention elsewhere,
the public of the settlement was not concerned to
enquire. They merely recognised the fact, and
yawned.
“Things demned slow!” mumbled Surgeon
Slipshott to Ensign Chick, as the latter met the
doctor crossing the lane to the soldier’s condemned-
cell. The military officer was on his rounds.
“ Yaas, ’tis so ! ” replied Chick, too exhausted
to speak, except in monosyllables.
“Things livelier up at th’ Island, s’pose?”
further interrogated Slipshott, not really anxious
for an answer, but feeling he must make some
effort to dispel the gloom which shrouded his
spirits.
“Ah, rather,” responded the boy, roused into
spasmodic intelligence by the allusion to Norfolk
Island, whence he had just returned. “ They do
do things up there. Hanged eighteen slick,
y’know, just ’fore I came down. Was on jury.”*
“H’m, yes, heard something o’ that. Well,
we’ve never a hanging here now. Things too
civilised. They all go up town. And these hot
days the prisoners don’t even exert themselves to
get flogged. Muggins, chief scourger, you know,
says his muscle’s getting quite soft—only flogged
two fellows this week !”
“What say—Muggins, here?”
“Yes; Argus brought him from Ross with him.
Says he’s only man in Establishment whose style
suits him.”
“ Ya-as ; heard that ’fore. Muggins nice touch
with the wrist. Saw him nick Egerton, mornin’
suicided.”
(Egerton, formerly Captain of the Onety-Onths,
and a convict attached to Ross Probation Gang,
being flogged by order of Superintendent Argus,
had, tne moment he had been freed from the
triangles, seized a soldier’s musket and shot him-
self.)
“Oh, were you there?”
“ Ya-as ; helped to pick him up.”
“Why—excuse the question—why didn’t you
interfere ?”
“Me !” Ensign Chick positively put some
energy into his response. “Why should I inter-
fere ?”
“Oh, don’t know, ’cept follow'd been brother-
officer, gentleman, and all that. You or lor any-
one high up in service can always beg such a chap
off.”
“ Well I did think of it, but, you see, doctor, I
was new to the System—just out —first post, and,
’sides .”
The youngster stopped short in his self-exculpa-
tory speech.
“ Besides what ?”
“The Chief Justice had sent a private note to
Argus that no favour was to be shown the man.
Egerton, or Dale, as fellow’s prison-name was,
had insulted the C.J. But you’ll keep that to
yourself. ”
“H’m, certainly. But—but, I must go and
see your man. See you later.”
And with a mutual salute the two officers
parted, the military to complete his rounds, and
the civilian to visit a soldier in the cell of the
battlemented tower, and to
“Blast the System !”
* * «
A very extraordinary observation to fall
from a trusted officer of the System ! But it was
in this respect appropriate, as Surgeon Slipshott
was an extraordinary officer.
Nature had done little and fortune less for Fred.
Slipshott. The one had endowed him with just
enough capacity and no more to obtain the
humblest license of the Society of Apothecaries,
and the latter with sufficient cash to pay his
hospital and lecture fees, for his diploma and his
brass-plate. Thus equipped, his professional
course had brought him within • measureable dis-
tance of starvation within a couple of years of his
“ qualification ” and his marriage, for, being penni-
less and unthrifty, he had, of course, at the earliest
possible opportunity, resolved to ease his burden
by sharing it with a wife.
The wolf, however, did not actually arrive at the
door. His wife’s aunt owned a distant connection
who was also a Right Honourable Connection and
had something to do with the colonies—ruled ’em,
in fact, by virtue of a profound ignorance of every-
thing concerning than. And the Great Man had
condescended on the seventy-seventh appeal to
“do something ’’ for the young and anything but
brilliant Esculapius.
“ Give that Slipshott,” he said to the Permanent
Under-Secretary, “ a medical superintendency on
a transport to Hobart Town in New South Wales.
And tell the Governor, whoever he is, to appoint
him to some post when he gets there.”
And so Slipshott, snapping at the billet as the
proverbial drowning-man snaps at the proverbial
straw, was, in due time, enrolled in Her Majesty’s
Service as Assistant-Surgeon, first to a Probation
Gang on Norfolk Bay, and later, to the infamous
Port Arthurites. The latter appointment meant
promotion, £2OO a year instead of £l5O, and a
pretty detached cottage instead of the two rooms
in a log hut, and, yet, the young doctor would
have refused it, but for the conviction that his
refusal would do him harm with the powers that
were.
“ Down here, little one,” he said to his wife, “ I
have some influence. Up at the Settlement I’ll
have none unless I fall in with their ways.”
“That you must never do, Fred,” was the
answer. “Better give up the Service than that!”
“I can’t give up the Service. I wish to God I
could ! But there’s no population out here to
give a new man practice, even if I had the
ability, and that, my dear, you know to your
sorrow, I have not.”
“ Hush ! Fred, or I shall get angry, if you talk
in that way.” And an attempt was made to stop
his mouth with a white, little hand. “If you
are—if you are not very —clever, you’re good
enough for me, dearest. ”
“ I’m good enough for the System, you mean.
And good for nothing else, and, therefore, I’ll
have to take this stop. I can’t quarrel with the
System. But I'm afraid I shall be powerless up
there to soften any of the pris’ners’ hardships.
Boote and Argus, the new super, have no
sympathy with that sort of thing. ”
“Yes, I’m afraid you must accept —we mustn’t
quarrel with the chiefs,” his wife asserted, with a
sigh. “But you won’t get hardened, I’m sure,
and while you keep sympathetic, there’ll always
be a chance of doing a little towards making some
poor wretch’s lot easier.”
“Well, we can but try. But there’ll be so
many eyes upon us there, and so many voices to
denounce an act of kindness as an act of weak-
ness, that we shall have to be careful.”
From which conversation the principal in-
ference to be drawn is that Mr. F. Slipshott,
L.S.A. by diploma and M.D. by courtesy, was
indeed an extraordinary officer—a very extra-
ordinary officer ! Inasmuch as it will be apparent
that he was consciously striving to obey the
System and at the same time to preserve a manly,
a humane and tender heart.
That he should fail was inevitable. Only a man
with a physical and moral backbone knitted in
the strongest fashion and an alert brain had the
slightest chance of success. And poor Slipshott,
though not badly furnished in body and soul was,
as we have hinted, very indifferently supplied in
the upper storey. As he watched, the unfolding
of the subtle immoralities and Satanic ingenuities
of the System, he was driven to despair by his
own incapacity.
“I’m a dashed failure, that’s what I am,” he
would groan into his wife’s ears. “I know when
those devils —for devils they are —I know that
they’re treating the men cruelly, but for the life o’
me I can’t see where they break the Reg’lations.
Take the case, now, of the pier chain-gang. The
Reg’lation is that the average load per man shall
not exceed lOOlbs., and that if an unusually heavy
log is to be carried, fresh men are to be put on
the chain. But when an overseer has a down on
a man, he can throw two hundredweight or over
on the fellow, and then, and then, there’s another
death through deceased’s own neglect!”
“But how, Ned —I don’t understand?”
“Well, this way, if my dunderheadedness will
let me make it clear to you. The Overseer will
order the pris’ner to the end coupling if the gang
are going uphill, but coming down he’ll transfer
the man to the first coupling, and as likely as not
fix him ‘unicorn,’ that is, without a mate. What
happens then ? The scoundrels at the end of the
chain purposely mis-step, the trunk lurches for-
ward, and the doomed wretch in the front has the
weight on his shoulder increased four or five-fold.
If he owns a giant’s strength he can sustain the
strain for a moment, long enough to wriggle from
underneath the log, and then he’s fortunate if he
escapes with a broken leg and a flogging. And
then he’s fortunate, because he gets six months in
hospital, and is let off with a flogging when he’s
all right again—he’s flogged, you know, for shirk-
ing his work in dropping the log ”
A horrified interjection from Mrs. Slipshott—
“Fred!”
“I’m speaking Gospel truth, wife. That’s where
he’s fortunate, I say, for if he has a mate, and
that other’s killed by the log, then the chances ara
all in favour of his being hanged.
“ And what if he hasn’t a giant’s strength ?”
“Then, he simply goes under. You see the
bending of the backs, and note the lurching rush
of the trunk as it’s propelled forward, and then
you hear a horrid ‘ Cr-s-s-sh /’ and you pick up a
mass of bloody flesh.”
“Don’t, dear ! I can’t—bear it!”
“ I thought you wanted to hear all about it ? I’ve
never told you these things before —not the worst
of them—and wouldn’t have gone intodetail to-day,
but you asked me. ’Never understand what a
woman means !”
“ Don’t be angry, Fred, but—it’s too horrible.
And, surely, there’re not many cases like that ?”
“No —not many !” the doctor answered, grimly.
“Average deaths per thousand of population last
year in colony, 15. At this Settlement and Pro-
bation Gangs on Tasman’s Peninsula, 35 per
thousand. Chain-gang death-rate, on an average
strength of sixty men— guess ?”
Mrs. Slipshott shook her head.
“ Fifteen —mostly ‘ accidents ’ such as I’ve
described, deaths under the lash, and gibbetings.
And fifteen deaths in the gang, is at the rate of
250 per thousand.”
“And the Governor, the Authorities, know
this ?”
“As I’ve mentioned to you often before, what
the Governor knows, the Comptrollers tell him.
And the Comptroller tells him what he chooses,
and the Commandant here tells the Comptroller
what he chooses, and the Overseer of the chain-
gang, and men in the like positions, tell the
Commandant what they choose. And if I were to
make a complaint about this logging game, the
Overseer would request the Commandant to have
the log which had killed a man measured, and its
contents divided by the number of prisoners on the
chain, and, of course, a result would be arrived at
which would show that the average weight did not
exceed the Regulation allotment. That’s what
gravels me. A smarter man would find a way to
stop their tricks, but so long as they keep to the,
letter of the Regulations, they beat me. A hun-
dred times a week I notice things occurring,
damnable things, shameful things, but I dare not
interfere, because the staff keep within the Regu-
lations or else within the limits of what the Com-
mandant considers as ‘a fair, discretionary
power.’F Good God ! I wish I had brains, or
money! But I can’t fight the System without. ”
* * #
So, possessing neither brains nor money ;
bound to retain his post at the Establishment as a
means of livelihood, poor, soft-headed and soft-
hearted Slipshott could hit upon no other expe-
dient with which to neutralise the ingenious
diabolisms of the System than hypocrisy.
In conversing with his brother-officers or those
of the military, he adopted the cynical and brutal
tone which it was the proper thing for both classes
to use in referring to convicts.
In addressing prisoners, in the presence of an
official, he would be as truculent, as unbelieving,
as blackguardly, as Superintendent Argus himself.
And he chose his favourites—every Penal Officer
had his favourite villain—from among the vilest
rascals. Which, of course, was also the correct
thing to do. The System did not necessarily hang
the worst criminals. It left a number alive to
fulfill the function of “ Officers’ pets.”
Surgeon Slipshott did all these things, and to
that extent hoodwinked and faithfully obeyed the
System. Only, when he came into direct and
unwatched intercourse with the prisoners, he
threw off his cynicism and brutality, and en-
deavoured to show himself to them as he was—as
a man of sympathetic nature who was sensible of
the iniquities of the existing conditions.

To he Immediately Published:
“In MIDDLE HAEBOUE and other Versoj”
(Chiefly Australian)
By THOMAS HENEY.
London : Kegan, Paul, Trench, Trtioner and Co
Price, Four .Shilling*.
In this volume, the writer has endeavoured to con-
vey his vivid impressions of the life and landscape
of the pastoral regions. Mindful of the admirable
work in such books as Mr. Farrell’s “How H e
Died,” and in the columns of some of the weekly
papers, the writer does not claim to have entered
an unoccupied field, but thinks it will be admitted,
bv those acquainted with the scenes and persons
of his verse, that it is faithful to the virile and
picturesque characteristics of the life of the station,
and the powerful and strange scenery of pas-
toral territory. in a series of eight rhymed
poems, in three blank-verse poems, and in other
verse, it is sought to suggest the dominant features
of this free and vigorous life. In “ A Squatter of
’GB,” an effort has been made to seize the dramatic
moments of the career of a pioneer squatter driven
under. “Pioneer” is an episode of the opening
of new country in South Queensland. Such poems
as “The Boundary Rider,” “At the Tank before
Day,” “The Groom’s Hut,”and “In the Lignum,"
are descriptive. Two poems, “The Shearer”
and “Found Dead,” outline two of the tragedies
of the bush. In these and other verses, the writer
has endeavoured to utilise his knowledge of local
colour derived from residence and travel. The
poem from which the book gets its name is a
lengthy blank-verse study of camping-out, in
which opportunity is taken to introduce a sketch
of one of the most striking incidents of bush-life.
Of the miscellaneous verse not otherwise specified,
suffice it to say it includes ten sonnets, and
several poems of lyrical movement. The contents
of the volume are so far new that they have not
previously been published. They will make a book
of about 112 pages, which can be procured through
the leading booksellers as soon as copies are re-
ceived from London. Of the writer’s previous
book, so competent a critic as Mr. Francis Adams
wrote in warm terms in the Queenslander of
4th December, 1880, reprinted from Brisbane
Courier of immediately previous date. This
review was re-published in the most important
Riverina newspapers.

Thus, he saved many a prisoner from receiving
a full Hogging, and sent numbers into hospital
when, perhaps, they suffered from nothing more
than the trifling ailments (as the System estimated
them) of a broken heart or a crushed spirit. This
weakness of his thrust very frequently double tides
of work upon him, for when ho ordered a man to
■ hospital whose suffering, while real, was not
tangible, he was compelled to find an excuse for
relieving his brother-surgeons of the patient’s care.
“Look here, Kerr,” he’d say to his senior,
“you look seedy, old man. Better run up town,
hadn’t you, for a week ? I’ll bolus your black-
guards for you.” And Dr. Kerr, nothing loth to
sneak a holiday, would admit to being out of
sorts, and would dissipate in Hobart Town just
long enough to enable Slipshott’s patient to pick
up new strength.
It was this readiness of Slipshott to assist both
his fellow-medicos and the creatures upon whom
tlie Law had fixed its fangs that had occasioned
that visit to the military dungeon, on making
which he had encountered Ensign Chick.
Gore, junior regimental surgeon of the 50th,
was on a visit to the capital, having arranged
with “ Slippy ” to look after the guards’ hospital.
Private Ernest Trench, of the 50th, having-
broken his sergeant-major’s spine with a musket
ball, and been, consequently, sentenced by court
martial to stand in front of a platoon at ten paces,
would have none of the religious consolations
which the Regiment and the System graciously
tendered him. All that he would consent to do
was to see that “ damned good-natured fool,
Croaker Slippy.”
Some of the keener-witted prisoners had taken
Slipshott’s measure, and in a quiet way had trans-
mitted an account of his real character to the
soldiers’ cantonments. Thus, when Private
Trench, duly incarcerated in the cell at the base
of the battlemented tower which was exclusively
reserved for the military condemned, insulted first
one clergyman and then the other, his comrades
urged him to send for Slipshott.
“ You’ll want to send some messages to the folks
at ’ome, won’t you, Trench ?” his corporal had
suggested. “ An’ as ye won’t see the parsons, an’
’ate the subaltern, ye’ll ’ave to see the Croaker, or
uo’un.”
* * *
The doctor found the condemned soldier
sitting on his stretcher, manacled and fettered.
Trench’s face bore the lines of fine breeding, over-
lain by the sodden aspect of the sot.
“You want to see me, my man?” Slipshott
asked, as he motioned the sentry to close the cell-
door.
“ Yes, doctor,” replied Trench. “But, please
drop the professional and the superior official for
a few moments, if you can, and let me speak to
you as man to man.”
The doctor, though naturally surprised at an
address of this sort from a common private,
nodded assent. Trench’s words had the purity of
accent of an educated man, and, coupled with the
evidence of his features, revealed to Dr. Slipshott
that he had to do with the dissolute and broken-
down “swell.”
“Thanks, doctor. I suppose you understand
by this time that I did not always belong to the —
h’m —‘ rankers ’?”
The surgeon nodded again.
“Well, I shall be obliged if you’ll do me a
favour—you’re the only man in the Settlement I’d
ask.”
“I’ll do it if I can, and if it involves no par-
ticular breach of duty.”
“ It’s very simple. You know there’s a party
of visitors coming from Hobart Town to view this
pretty show-place, and the turtle-doves and
domestic pets caged here ?”
“I know some English ladies and gentlemen
are coming. They’ll be here shortly, if they’re
not here already. The signal showed they were
at the head of Norfolk Bay two hours ago. The
tramway and boat will have brought ’em here by
this. But what about ’em ? ! ”
“ Don’t let them see me ! That's my request.”
The Doctor thought the private’s appeal a very
natural one. Nothing caused the hundreds of
educated criminals at the Settlement greater pain
or a more shameful humiliation than the occa-
sional inspection by curious visitors. Slipshott
was, however, doubtful of his capacity to prevent
the tourists viewing the tower, one of the show-
spots of Port Arthur, and said so.
“ I don’t see how I can. The Commandant is
proud of the architecture of this tower, and,
besides, the view from the battlements is particu-
larly grand.”
“ You must, Dr. Slipshott. Say I’m suffering
from infectious disease, or so desperate that more
murder’ll be done if I’m to be trotted out for their
inspection.”
“ That’s nonsense, my good m—l mean, Trench.
That would make ’em more anxious to see you, and
the officer of the day would merely strengthen the
guards, and put forty-pound irons on you.”
“ Neither would stop me from getting at him,”
hissed the soldier, “not even though she was
standing by.” And he beat his manacled hands
against the pallet.
“What d’ye mean?” demanded the Surgeon,
amazed at the sudden passion displayed by the
doomed private as well as the strangeness of his
remark.
“Oh ! of course, you don’t know. How could
you know ? And I may as well tell you, though I
didn’t intend to have said a word. The reference
slipped out.” He paused for a second and then re-
sumed : “ You have heard who these people are?”
“ I did hear, but have forgotten. All I remem-
ber is that a Member of Parliament and his
daughter are in the lot.”
“ Well, I knotu and it. was the knowledge that
made me shoot the non-com. The House of Com-
mons man is named Ernest Trench Leppell.
That’s my name, too. Now do you understand?”
“ Good God ! But your name is Trench, isn’t
it?”
“No! I dropped the surname when I’listed,
of course. The name’s the same. The old scoun-
drel’s my godfather—and my uncle—him. And
the girl with him. God bless her, is—is my cousin,
and was (the tragic bitterness of that was !) en-
gaged to be my wife. Now, will you sanction them
poking me up and inspecting me, for the wild
beast that I am ?”
“ I’ll —try —” said the Surgeon, still more
astounded.' “ That is, if you give me your wo'rd
you are .”
“Telling the truth? Heavens, man! Why
should I lie to you now ? Do you think I’d care
if it were myself or that scoundrel only to be con-
sidered ? Why, I’d rejoice that he should see me,
so that I could taunt him. The sight and the
words should haunt his memory and burden his
conscience till his dying day. But it’s the girl—l
don’t wish to kill her ! She thinks I’m dead.”
The stupid and sympathetic surgeon, _ shocked
by this revelation, yet hadn’t the least idea how
to act.
“I’m very sorry, but I don’t see what I’m to
do. I’d do anything to help you, and the young
lady, but if the man is an M.P. he’ll want to see
everything.”
“ Of course he will! Don’t I know him ! And
he’ll be the more anxious to see everything because
of the disclosures about his mines.”
“What, is he Leppell, the mine-owner?”
“Yes, Leppell, the mine-owner—Leppell, em-
ployer of six and seven-year-old children whom he
harnessed with iron girdle and chain to coal-
trucks—Leppell, the Christian philanthropist —
Leppell, the Puritan, who cut his brother’s son
adrift because he loved wine, woman, and song—
that’s the Leppell who’s come to these parts to
inform himself of the Colonial and Penal ques-
tions ! He’s the Leppell who’ll go home and make
such a fuss about the colonies and the Depen-
dencies of the great Empire on which the sun
never sets, that people’ll forget about the children
who never saw the sunlight except on Sundays.
He’ll go home and report on the intense loyalty of
the colonies, and the perfection of the transporta-
tion system —Ugh ! That’s the Leppell. Now,
you’ll stop them coming? ”
“ I’ll do my best, then,” answered the Surgeon.
“But what did you mean when you said just now
that it was this man’s visit that made you shoot
the non-com.”
“This; I was just off sentry-duty at Eagle-
hawk Neck, and was crossing to quarters with
another private when we saw the semaphore arms
flying rapidly. T’other fellows cried out; ‘ Race
you up, Trench, to see what the signal is ! ’ We
slung the muskets, and started uphill to the signal-
post. Of course, we’d no right there, and _no
right to know the signal, but if the chiefs think
either the military or the prisoners don’t know
every message that’s sent to and from the city
they’re greatly mistaken. I got to the top first,
though I was pretty puffy from the previous
night’s drinking—the officers ’ll tell you I was
never sober ; but that’s a lie, I was rarely—and the
convict in charge read the signal to me as he was
passing it on to the Long Bay. God ! I remember
the way how the fellow mumbled and mouthed the
words.”
He mimicked the slow utterance of the signal-
man : “ English Sydney—visitors coming—
inspect—Settlement in—two—three —days. Have
—everything—order—as—party—includes—mem-
ber—House —Commons —Leppell—daughter ”
“ I listened up to that last word,” Trench con-
tinued, after a breathless space, “and the whole
thing flashed upon me—what it would mean to me
—and her, if she should see me. For you must
know, doctor, she’d have married me in spite of
all my wildness, but she wouldn’t disobey the old
man.
“And then I rushed downhill—mad. I was
mad ; lam sure of it. For a few minutes—mad —
till I’d shot the non-com.”
“But what had he to do with the matter or
them ?”
“ I’m coming to that. Nothing ! I went towards
the huts under the sand-hummocks, with seven
devils inside of me crying furious’y for revenge !
blood ! And then, as Satan would have it, the
Sergeant-Major came round the hummock. I had
no grievance against him —none ! He was the
best friend I had in the regiment—used often to
keep me straight—but the devils were in me !
“He turned to look at the Semaphore, which
was still busy—telling the Settlement to put all
thS ugly things out of sight, I suppose ! —and his
back was towards me. And I thought what a
splendid target he’d make—and wished to heaven
it was man ancle, and —and the next thing I
remember is feeling quite calm and collected,
holding the smoking musket in my hand and
seeing the Sergeant-Major in front o’ me lying
prone. I had shot him in my mad spasm. For
you, as a medical man, will perhaps agree that I
.was mad.”
A shuddering nod of acquiescence was given by
the Doctor. It was a case like hundreds made
familiar to him by his experiences under the
System. A prisoner would bear up for years
against the pressure of the penal regime, and then
would commit some act of apparently purposeless
atrocity which became explicable only when it
could be attributed to a sudden lesion of the
brain.
“ Why did you not tell something of this to the
Court-martial ? ”
“ What could I have told that they'd have
believed? And it’s just as well as it is. Had I
been free, and been on guard when my uncle
came to visit the Wreck, I believe, on my soul,
that I’d have shot him. And that would ha’ been
bad for my—for the girl. No ! —it’s best as it is.
My only hope now is that neither’ll hear or see
anything of me. ”
A hurry of feet on the pave, and a rattle of
muskets and door-chains interrupted the talk.
Slipshott had only time to whisper to the Con-
demned that he would do all in his power to meet
his wishes, and to press his hand, when Ensign
Chick appeared at the cell-entrance.
“ Oh, you here still, Slipshott? Just come to
see if everything’s shipshape for the visitors.
They’ll want to see you, Trench, my man, so be
on your best behaviour. There’s a Member of
Parliament among ’em and we want a good
report. ”
“Take my advice, Chick,” said the Surgeon
with an assumption of grimness, “clear out of
here yourself, and don’t let the visitors come any-
where about. Trench is suffering from —ah —
smallpox.”
Ensign Chick backed away, aghast. “Good
God ! Are you sure?”
Slipshott nodded, and Chick hastened away to
report.
Trench laughed as the door closed. “ A good
thought, doctor.”
“it may be too good,” rejoined Slipshott.
“ They may hurry on the ”
“The execution—well, so much the better.”
* # #
That was precisely what did happen. The
original intention was to platoon Private Trench
the next morning at seven o’clock. The Com-
mandant, who had returned from the capital with
the party of distinguished visitors, on hearing that
the condemned soldier was suffering from smallpox,
begged the Captain of the detachment to execute
him that evening, and head-quarters approval of
the Court Martial’s finding having been received
by the same boat that brought the visitors, the
Captain signified his willingness to oblige the
Commandant.
“Not the least objection personally,” he said.
“It’s only a question of the prisoner himself, and
the parsons.”
“Oh, you needn’t trouble, sir, about them, L
think,” exclaimed Ensign Chick. “Private
Trench grossly insulted both chaplains.”
“Dear, dear!” joined in Mr. Leppell, M.P.,
who was standing by, “ what a desperate offender
he.must be. Did you say his name was Trench ? ”
“Yes, sir—Private Trench.”
“He, lie ! how funny. Trench is one of my
names. And you are really going to execute him
this evening ?
“Yes, sir—six o’clock.”
“ I’m engaged to dine with you, Mr. Comman-
dant, I think —I and my ladies. Shall I have
time to dress after the execution, for—he, he ! —I
should like to sec it ? ”
And he did. _
PRICE WARUNG.
Effeminate Literature.
“Mb. Hall Caine,” said a London interviewer
to the author of “The Bondman,” “have you
read Grant Allen’s recent ouburst in Ihe
Speaker against the bloodthirstiness of the taste
of the English people in literature, art, and
drama ?”
“Oh, yes,” said Mr, Caine, “I read Grant
Allen, but I do not agree either with him or his
critics. They rose in wrath and pointed to notable
novelists in whom there was said to bo no such
passion for blood. Well, the very names they
used convicted them, and they ignored several of
the most popular. But why should not literature
be bloodthirsty ? What is the offence ? Scott,
Richardson, Fielding, all the great literature of
the past, has been so. Remember this ; When the
taste in fiction is not for blood, it is nearly always
for something worse—sensuality. Which of the
two is the more unwholesome —which would we
wish most to repudiate—the sensuality of France
and its literature, or the noble bloodthirsti-
ness of the old Sagas, of the Greeks, of
our heroic English ? Just look around and
see what is going on in English art, litera-
ture, drama. Instead of too much blood there
is too little, if it be the alternative for
lust. It is always lust now. Take the drama
and look at the pieces going. ‘The Village
Priest’ turns on a base alliance. In ‘ The Man’s
Shadow,’ the pivot is that one of the two husbands
thinks his wife is disloyal with his bosom friend.
These plays of French origin are nearly always
sheer stories of lust. Perhaps you say, ‘To the
pure, all things,’ &c. Wretched cant, sii’, in this
connection! There is at least something virile
and manly in bloodthirstiness, there is nothing
but what is basest, and cruelest, and meanest in
sensuality. Look at ‘The Dead Heart,’ ‘La
Tosca,’ ‘Partners,’ ‘ The Profligate,’a noble play
—but still lust, lust,_ lust. The whole scope and
scheme, aim and direction of French literature
in nearly evei'y novel that makes itself felt is
toward lust—Zola, Daudet, Paul Bourget, Flau-
bert, the Goncourts. English literature is a thou-
sand times purer, and its purest part is that which
is most bloodthirsty. There is blood enough
in ‘Lorna Doone,’ and yet the book is purity
itself. ‘ The Master of Ballantrae ’ is also
bloody, but pure. Black’s best book, ‘ Macleod of
Dare,’ends with murder and suicide. When you
come to modern English books with no blood in
them, they are often either insipid or impure. I
never pick up the reviews without finding that the
plots of more than half the new novels turn on
illicit love. This running away from blood is an
effeminate thing. There is too little blood flowing
nowadays through English literature. And this I
take to be a sign of the effeminate spirit of the
times. It shows itself in the worship of such a
man as Shelley, and ‘Ouida’ and Grant Allen
are brave enough to tell us that any woman would
have been honored if Shelley had made her his
mistress or trodden her under his feet. They say,
in effect, ‘ Would it not be a distinction to be
seduced by Goethe?’ This utter unmanliness
makes one'sick. It reminds me of the story of the
little Italian coxcomb who used to introduce his
Avife as ‘ a past mistress of the celebrated Lord
Byron.’ The temper of mind of that little man
was laughed at fifty years ago, but now it
seems to be imitated. It has come to be
thought that a man of genius can make
other claims upon his fellow-creatures than
are proper to men of the world. A man of
genius should be required to behave as other men
behave, or else be kicked. For pity’s sake, let us
banish this effeminacy which leads to so much
silly talk and silly ivriting. ”
“But,” urged the interviewer, “do you not
think that to a certain extent Avomen writers are
to blame for this sort of thing ? They seem to be
the greatest offenders.”
“Certainly they are,” Mr. Caine replied ; “the
woman author is a thing of our own generation.
There have been Fanny Burney, and Mrs.
Radcliffe, and Lady Blessington, in time past,
Avho Avrote and published, but they did not make
it the regular business of life. Well, Alien women
became professional litterateurs, they had to do
Avhat they could —not always what they would—
and their experiences did not include adventures,
fights, battles, duels; in short, they knew
nothing of blood and could write nothing
about it. What they did knoAv, if they
Avere healthy-minded women, Avas Avhat Jane
Austen kneAV—the manners, whims, eccentricities
of polite society. If they Avere women of un-
healthy instincts, what they kneAV were the secret
and base complications of sexual passion. Is it
not a common remark that half the books Avhieh
deal —not with struggles leading to blood,
with base passions—are written by Avomen ? Even
Charlotte Bronte, a woman Avho Avas half a man in
the temper of her mind and character —even her
chief book is on this theme. ‘ Jane Eyre ’is a
story, magnificently told, of one of the meanest,
basest, ugliest complications it is possible to think
of. There you have, perhaps, the noblest and
finest example of what a Avoman with her neces-
sarily limited experience of life could do. But
the more umvorthy of her imitators, who could
not imagine such a complication as the spiritual
tragedy of Jane’s own life, and could only invent
the squalid relation of Rochester and his mad
Avife, have gone on producing out of evil ex-
perience these base amours. I agree very largely
with Count Lyof Tolstoi, in his ‘Kreutzer
Sonata,’ and hold that the love-passion, both on
its spiritual and on its sensual sides, is exalted by
modern writers to such undue importance that it
seems to consume the best energies of man. If
Ave believe the novels and plays of the time, there
is next to nothing in life but love, and next to
nothing in love but lust. LoA ; e is a part, not the
Avhole, of life. But it so dominates literature now
that if it Avas forbidden to dramatists and novelists
to touch upon the illicit side of love, nearly all the
theatres would be closed and not a hundredth
part of the novels would be written. Our time of
prolonged peace has something to be charged with
in this relation. In a peaceful age the true virility
of men seems to be drained away. The placid
unhealthiness of peace nearly always reacts in-
juriously on literature. No, I say, if literature
must either be bloody or sensual, then ‘ Long life
to Bloodthirstiness ! ’ ”
